

2005 WL 698411 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELISANDRO GERVACIO SOLANO MARIBEL GERVACIO HERNANDEZ
Files: A96 052 760 - Los Angeles
A96 052 761
March 9, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Emilio Amaya
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered orally on November 5, 2004. The appeal was accordingly due on or before December 6, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was mailed after the due date and filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on January 14, 2005. We accordingly find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal the respondents claim ineffecitve assistance of counsel, asserting that prior counsel failed to file their appeal. However, the respondents have failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondents have failed to submit a detailed statement of their agreement with Fred Browne, there is no evidence that the respondents informed prior counsel of the allegations against him, and it does not appear that the California Bar complaint was actually filed1. Nor have the respondents established that they were prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000). Therefore, we do not find this claim to be an adequate reason to consider this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

An original, signed complaint form is contained in the record. There is no evidence of mailing or filing or a response from the state bar.

2005 WL 698424 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DEL CARMEN GARCIA-LEAL LAURO LEAL-MARTINEZ
Files: AXX XX9 990 - Los Angeles
AXX XX9 991
March 8, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Steven R. Espinoza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Remand; cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Act
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondents have appealed the Immigration Judge’s September 25, 2003, decision to deny their request for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The respondents argue that we should remand the record because of their prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondents’ motion because there is no evidence that the respondents’ former counsel has been informed of the charges against her and given an opportunity to respond. The respondents contend, by way of a vague declarative statement, that they notified their prior counsel of the charges by telephone.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, recently issued a decision regarding Lozada’s notice requirement. In Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003), the alien sent a letter regarding the ineffectiveness of the alien’s prior counsel to the California State Bar. The letter included a line stating “cc” and the prior counsel’s name. Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. The court observed that the “cc” notation, at best, indicated that the alien intended on sending the prior counsel notice of the state disciplinary proceedings, but that the respondent offered no evidence that he sent the prior counsel notice of the state disciplinary proceedings. Id. at 1131. Furthermore, the court noted that the alien failed to give the prior counsel notice of his motion to reopen. Id. at 1131-1132. The court emphasized that the purpose of Lozada’s notice requirement is to encourage the prior counsel to contest the allegations of ineffective assistance so that the adjudicator can more accurately assess the claim of ineffective assistance. Id. at 1132. Inasmuch as the alien provided no evidence that the prior counsel received the notice of the motion to reopen and did not give the prior counsel an opportunity to respond to the allegations, the court held that the alien failed to comply with Lozada’s notice requirement. Id.
 
*2 In the instant case, neither the respondents’ letter to the disciplinary authorities nor any affidavit from the respondents themselves or their current counsel indicate that a copy was sent to the respondents’ prior counsel. We also note that the respondents have not provided an affidavit regarding any communications with their prior counsel. Therefore, we find that the mere assertion that notice was given without any other evidence is insufficient to establish that the respondents substantially complied with Lozada’s notice requirement. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. Inasmuch as the respondents do not appeal any other portion of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the appeal is dismissed and the motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to so depart, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698476 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVINDER PAUL
File: AXX XX3 114 - Los Angeles
March 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Helen A. Sklar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on August 16, 2002, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s applications for relief. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen these proceedings. The motion is denied.
 
We find that the respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on November 15, 2004, is untimely. Motions to reopen removal proceedings must be filed within 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the present case, this Board rendered the final decision in these proceedings on August 16, 2002. Therefore, under the strict language of the regulations, the respondent’s motion was due no later than November 14, 2002. However, we acknowledge that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time limitations for motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent argues that the time limitation on his motion to reopen should be tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge and the Board. In the present case, the respondent was represented by Surjit Singh during his removal proceedings. After the Board’s decision of August 16, 2002, the respondent hired new counsel, Vera A. Weisz, who filed a Petition for Review with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals on September 16, 2002. This petition was denied on June 23, 2004. Subsequently, the respondent hired present counsel on August 17, 2004, who filed the motion on November 15, 2004, claiming that the respondent received ineffective assistance from his prior counsels.
 
Although the respondent claims that his applications for relief were denied solely because Mr. Singh failed to prepare the respondent for the removal hearings and failed to obtain additional corroborating evidence, a review of the record indicates that the asylum application was denied due to inconsistencies and omissions in the respondent’s testimony, which led to an adverse credibility finding in this case (I.J. at 11-12; 16). In addition, the respondent testified that he was unable to obtain some of the corroborating evidence because it was not available to him (I.J. at 18). After our affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent was on notice that the evidence he submitted in support of his claim was deficient. The respondent did not raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Mr. Singh until after the Petition for Review had been denied by the Court of Appeals, and he realized that his attorney’s tactical approaches failed. We find that it is inappropriate to toll the statute of limitations under these circumstances. Therefore, we conclude that the respondent’s motion was untimely. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; see also Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.

2005 WL 698471 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TCHERNO DJAMUGUE
File: AXX XX6 166 - New York
March 3, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ashley Mammo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard VanVeldhuisen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on May 24, 2004, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 20, 2003, for failure to file a brief in accordance with 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(D). On January 12, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his first attorney due to that counsel’s failure to file an appeal brief, and requesting reopening on the basis of changed country conditions in Guinea. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
First, the motion to reopen is untimely. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on May 24, 2004. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before August 24, 2004. The record reflects that the respondent’s motion was filed on January 12, 2005. Consequently, the respondent’s motion is not properly before us for consideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Second, even assuming arguendo we assumed jurisdiction, the motion would be denied. We consider that although the respondent has generally complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), he has not shown that his former counsel acted ineffectively. He argues that his former counsel was obligated to file the appeal brief and provided ineffective assistance when he failed to do so. The Board, however, has held that satisfying the above procedural requirements is in and of itself an insufficient reason to reopen, and that an attorney’s failure to file an appeal on behalf of an alien is not per se ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Id. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id.
 
The respondent has failed to identify the substantive arguments he would have raised on appeal but for counsel’s ineffectiveness which prevented him from fully presenting his appellate arguments. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002) (ineffective assistance of counsel is a due process violation if the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his or her case due to fundamental unfairness of the proceedings). Consequently, although the respondent has generally met the requirements of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, supra, he has not shown that he suffered prejudice that affected the outcome of his case. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
*2 Finally, we find that the respondent has not proffered any material new evidence that was previously unavailable, as to changed country conditions in Guinea, or in any other regard, in support of his motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1242.22, 1003.2, and 1003.8. Moreover, he has not supported his motion with any applications for relief from removal and deportation, including asylum, and has failed to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief requested. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 470-474 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698449 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LU, ZHIRU
File: AXX XX8 013 - Los Angeles
March 3, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Swaim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sabrina Hall,
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves to reopen and remand the proceedings for consideration of an application for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. Although this motion is untimely, the respondent submits it should be excused from the usual time limitations because she had ineffective assistance of counsel from attorney Daniel W. Son, Jr. Although the respondent has included with this motion a detailed affidavit stating what problems she encountered with her former attorney, a copy of the letter her current attorney wrote to Mr. Son, and Mr. Son’s reply letter, she also contends in this motion that she cannot fully comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). She explains that the Texas Rules of Disciplinary Procedure prohibit any disclosure about whether a claim has been filed. Motion to Reopen at 7.
 
Holding aside the fact that this motion already appears to compromise the respondent’s interpretation of the Texas rule stated above, we find that the respondent has failed to prove she was prejudiced by Mr. Son’s representation. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999). The evidence does not prove her prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. As an initial matter, the record does not contain a copy of the I-130 that was filed on the respondent’s behalf or an application for relief (I-485). Further, the qualifying marriage took place during proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 245.1(c)(9). Finally, there is an adverse credibility finding in the record, bearing on discretion (I.J. at 6). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, we hold that reopening is not warranted.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698451 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAULO ROBERTO RAMIREZ-MATA
File: A92 313 294 - Los Angeles
March 3, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Edgardo Quintanilla, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Deborah Workeneh
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on October 26, 2000, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s December 8, 1998, decision finding the respondent removable as charged, and statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal. The respondent now moves the Board to reopen his case pursuant to recently issued regulations allowing special motions to seek relief under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996), for aliens who pleaded guilty or nolo contendere to certain crimes. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44.1 The respondent argues in his motion that, because he entered his plea agreement in February 1997, he is eligible to seek relief under 212(c) of the Act. The regulations, with certain exceptions not here applicable, specifically bar section 212(c) relief to an alien who has been charged and found removable on the basis of an aggravated felony. See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(f)(4). The respondent was charged and found removable on the basis of an aggravated felony, and he has not shown that his plea agreement was made prior to April 24, 1996. Accordingly, the respondent is ineligible for a section 212(c) waiver and the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

While the respondent, in a supplemental brief, requests that, in adjudicating his special motion to seek relief under section 212(c), we consider his arguments as to whether the Immigration Judge’s decision was in error, proceedings pursuant to special motions to seek relief under section 212(c) are solely for the purpose of adjudicating the application for section 212(c) relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(e).
Further, in his original appeal, the respondent did not dispute that his crime was an aggravated felony under the amended aggravated felony definition. Importantly, there are substantial indications in the record that the conviction was for residential burglary and the respondent has not complied with our requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. For us to even consider entertaining an untimely motion as to removability, the respondent would need to make an affirmative showing that his conviction would not be deemed an aggravated felony, such as by proffering his plea transcript showing that this burglary was not of a residence. See Matter of Beckford, 22, I&N Dec. 124 (BIA 2000).

2005 WL 698444 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GOLAM RABBEE CHOWDHURY
File: AXX XX9 229 - New York
March 2, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claudia Slovinsky, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying a motion to reopen on July 30, 2003. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board on November 9, 2004. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
Underlying the motion is a claim of ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former counsel. However, we cannot excuse the lateness of the motion in this case. In Iavorski v. INS, 124, 127, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000), the court suggested that the filing deadline for motions with the Board may be tolled based on deceptive actions of counsel. However, such tolling is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. See Tho Dinh Tran v. Alphonse Hotel Corp., 281 F.3d 23, 36-37 (2d Cir. 2002). Here, the respondent fails to show that he exercised diligence in pursuing his rights. The pending motion was filed over a year and 3 months after our last decision in the case. The respondent, while complaining about counsel who represented him at the time of our last decision, does not argue that counsel failed to inform him of that decision. Moreover, the respondent does not state when he consulted current counsel (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 21; Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 25).
 
In any event, we will not favorably exercise our discretion, where the respondent failed to report for removal as ordered. In its opposition to reopening, the DHS states that the respondent was ordered to report for removal on July 11, 2000, but he did not appear. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 257 (BIA 1985) (finding that an alien in deportation proceedings who fails to report to the Service following notification to report for immediate deportation does not merit the favorable exercise of discretion required for reopening); see also Bar-Levy v. INS, 990 F.3d 33, 35 (2d Cir. 1995)(holding that an alien who had failed to surrender for deportation was a “fugitive from justice” and was barred as a result of his fugitive status from calling upon the resources of the court to examine his claims). Counsel states in a letter that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel during this period. However, there is nothing from the respondent himself to indicate that he reasonably thought that he could ignore the order to report for removal. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698428 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BLANCA ELVIRA GUALIN RAMERIZ
File: AXX XX3 271 - Los Angeles
March 2, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her application for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). Based on the respondent’s credible testimony and the statements on her written application, we reject the Immigration Judge’s finding that she failed to satisfy the “good moral character” requirement for the relief. See sections 240A(b)(1)(B) of the Act. See also Vera- Villegas v. INS, 330 F.3d 1222, 1225, 1230-31 (9th Cir. 2003). For the reasons set forth in the decision, however, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent failed to sustain her burden of proof with regard to the “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” requirement for the relief. See sections 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent further contests the fairness of the removal hearings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. She has failed, however, substantially to comply with the procedural requirements for such a claim. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061(9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). She has also failed to put forth arguments and evidence to demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s alleged mishandling of her case. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 698302 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS IBARRA VASQUEZ
File: A96 340 637 - Los Angeles
February 28, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated December 16, 2004.1 In our prior decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“the Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), based on the fact that the respondent did not have a qualifying relative for purposes of section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent contends that he does, in fact, have a United States citizen child but did not believe that he could claim the child as a qualifying relative because the respondent’s name was not correct on the child’s birth certificate. In support of his motion, the respondent has submitted a copy of the child’s birth certificate, which lists the respondent’s name as “Luis Ibarra Vasques,” rather than “Jose Luis Ibarra Vasquez.” The respondent maintains that he has filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior attorney as a result of this error.
 
We find that the respondent has failed to establish a basis for reopening proceedings. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Specifically, the respondent has failed to establish that the evidence he seeks to offer was not available and could not have been discovered or presented during proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, we do not find the respondent’s explanation that he did not believe he could claim the child due to the discrepancy on the birth certificate to be reasonable, particularly in light of the fact that the respondent was specifically questioned by the Immigration Judge as to whether he had any United States citizen children. See Tr. at 7-8. Furthermore, as the respondent has not alleged any specific hardship to the United States citizen child, he cannot establish prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. See generally INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
Turning to the respondent’s claim against his former counsel, we find that the respondent has not meaningfully attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000). The only evidence that the respondent has even attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, is a copy of a complaint form purportedly filed with the State Bar of California. The complaint form, however, is incomplete and does not describe the nature of the respondent’s complaint against his former counsel. Furthermore, the respondent has failed to establish that he was prejudiced by the actions of his former counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent has not claimed that his prior counsel was even aware of the existence of his alleged United States citizen child, nor has he claimed that he was advised by prior counsel not to reveal the existence of the child as a result of the error on the birth certificate.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent characterizes his motion as a motion to reconsider, the motion is, in substance, a motion to reopen and has been adjudicated in accordance with the regulations governing such motions. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).

2005 WL 698292 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EMMANUEL BLANCA PAEZ
File: AXX XX7 985 - Los Angeles
February 25, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening and reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This matter was last before us on June 30, 1999, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from a decision of an Immigration Judge dated October 10, 1997, denying his motion to reopen deportation proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of deportation entered in his case on October 1, 1996. The respondent has submitted what is styled as a motion to reconsider and reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
Initially, if treated as a motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion is untimely filed. The regulations provide that a motion to reconsider in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 30 days after the date of that decision, or on or before July 31, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The Board denied the respondent’s appeal on June 30, 1999. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the respondent’s motion until December 28, 2004. If treated as a motion to reconsider, the motion is not properly before us for adjudication, as it was untimely filed.
 
Moreover, even if it had been timely submitted, the motion would have been denied. When deciding a motion to reconsider, the Board considers the case as though the decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered. If the respondent was eligible for relief at the time of the original decision, then in reconsidering the decision, we treat his status as that which it had been at the time of the initial decision. “The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). The respondent’s motion alleges error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent had the opportunity on appeal to raise all alleged errors in the Immigration Judge’s decision. However, the respondent raised no arguments on appeal which were not addressed by the Immigration Judge or which would have caused us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995), (proper service of notice of service or receipt of the notice by the respondent is not required to effect service). Because the respondent has not shown that our previous decision was defective in any way, the motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
Furthermore, if the respondent’s motion were treated as one to reopen, denial would still be appropriate. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 8, 2001. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before September 30, 1999. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the respondent’s motion until December 28, 2004. Therefore, the motion to reopen was filed out of time.
 
*2 Notwithstanding, even assuming arguendo the respondent’s motion had been timely filed, denial would still be appropriate. A “motion to reopen proceedings seeks to reopen proceedings so that new evidence can be presented and so that a new decision can be entered, normally after a further evidentiary hearing.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 403 (BIA 1991). Such a motion may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Finally, an alien must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought before a motion to reopen will be granted. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). Here, the respondent reiterates arguments that were made, and considered, below.
 
Furthermore, to the extent the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has failed to meet the threshold requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as articulated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). We recognize that the present case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that the procedural requirements established in Lozada are not sacrosanct. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F. 3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). However, aside from the respondent’s declaration that is appended to his motion and his contention that he has communicated with said former attorney and the Bar disciplinary authorities in Illinois concerning his former attorney’s actions, the respondent has not presented any evidence to corroborate these claims.
 
Moreover, the respondent waited more than five years to file this motion and does not allege any fraud or deception on the part of his former attorney that could possibly invoke consideration of “equitable tolling” of the statute. See Ituribarria v. INS, 321 F. 3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, the invoking party must show that he was prevented from properly filing a motion to reopen because of fraud, deception, or error); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F. 3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (in order for equitable tolling to apply, respondent needs to show that his ignorance was caused by circumstances beyond his control, and which go beyond a claim of excusable neglect.). The arguments contained in the respondent’s motion were previously considered, and rejected. Consequently, where the respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are compelled to deny the motion to reopen on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*3 Therefore, as the respondent offers no new or previously unavailable evidence, we find reopening is not warranted. Accordingly, the respondent’s motions are denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698298 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MENDOZA DELGADO
File: A75 623 493 - Los Angeles
February 24, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Phuong D. Mai, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Debra H. Workeneh
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 3, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s motion to reopen our decision of June 1, 2004, in which we affirmed without opinion an Immigration Judge’s March 18, 2003, decision to deny the respondent’s cancellation of removal application. The respondent was granted the privilege of voluntary departure. The respondent now has filed a motion to reconsider. The DHS has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and be supported by any pertinent precedent decisions. 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(b)(1) (2004). A motion to reconsider based upon a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied. See Matter of Tiwari, 20 I&N Dec. 254 (BIA 1991), citing Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216 (BIA 1990, 1991).
 
By way of background, an Immigration Judge had determined that the respondent had failed to establish exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his United States citizen children, and he therefore denied the cancellation application. In the respondent’s previous motion, which we denied on September 3, 2004, he had requested reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and he also requested additional time to present supplemental evidence bearing on the issue of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his children. In our decision denying the motion to reopen, we noted that the respondent had failed to comply with the requirements to prevail under an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, nor had he demonstrated that his prior counsel’s actions had prejudiced his cancellation application. In addition, we found that the respondent had not established that the additional evidence he sought to present in support of his cancellation application was previously unavailable.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent, through counsel, requests that the Board reconsider its previous decision. Counsel essentially alleges that it was error not to grant the respondent additional time to submit a “brief” after he filed his motion to reopen, and that we erroneously found that the respondent had not filed his motion to reopen within 90 days.1
 
*2 We decline to reconsider the respondent’s case. Upon review, we find that the respondent has not alleged the existence of any errors of law or fact in our prior decision to warrant reconsideration. In this connection, we point out that all relevant information should have been contained in the motion to reopen, and that the Board’s motions procedure does not allow additional time for the filing of a “brief” after the motion has been filed. Furthermore, counsel has incorrectly alleged that we denied the motion to reopen as untimely. Rather, in our decision of September 3, 2004, we found that 90 days already had elapsed since the entry of our decision on June 1, 2004, and that the respondent was therefore precluded from submitting additional evidence because the time period for filing the motion to reopen had expired. In any event, the respondent has failed to establish that his prior counsel’s actions adversely affected his cancellation application, or that the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his cancellation application because of the absence of any exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his United States citizen children was in error.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

To the extent that counsel’s instant motion could be construed as a motion to reopen, for purposes of completeness, we note that the instant motion represents an improper second motion to reopen that does not fall within any of the narrow exceptions to the regulation permitting only one motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).

2005 WL 698364 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS ALFONSO ORTIZ-RAYGOZA
File: A72 681 220 - San Francisco
February 18, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank P. Sprouls, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lucia A. Fiorentino,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen and remand proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 New counsel for the respondent claims that prior counsel failed to advise him that he was eligible for “re-papering,” an administrative device permitting certain aliens to be placed in removal proceedings so that they could pursue applications for cancellation of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
We have held that to satisfy a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must support his claim by a detailed affidavit, inform his previous attorney of the allegations of misconduct and provide former counsel with an opportunity to respond, and state whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the attorney’s alleged violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also, Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (Courts have held in similar situations that an alien was not required to fulfill procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, inasmuch as the record indicated a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance). Here, the respondent has failed to offer any evidence in support of his claim therefore the respondent has failed to satisfy any of the elements required to demonstrate that he was denied effective assistance of counsel. The respondent has submitted a photocopy of a letter filed with the State Bar of California, but the letter fails to provide the nature of the alleged ineffective assistance. Resp’s Motion, Exh. H. The respondent has not submitted any evidence that former counsel was contacted or that he was provided an opportunity to respond. We find that the respondent has failed to satisfy a showing of ineffective assistance. Moreover, the respondent has demonstrated that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance. Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent indicated that he became prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal due to the birth of his United States citizen child, but he has not presented any argument that his removal would cause his child the requisite hardship. See section 240A of the Immigration and Nationality Act.. Based upon our finding that the respondent was not provided ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s motion is denied as untimely filed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely filed, the time limitation period at section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act may be equitably tolled under the case law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. See Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002) (the limitation period on motions to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be tolled until petitioner was aware of the harm resulting from attorney’s misconduct). According to the respondent’s affidavit submitted in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, the respondent became aware of his former attorney’s initial ineffective assistance only after the respondent consulted new counsel in December 2004.

2005 WL 698359 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELOY CAMPOS-HERRERA
File: AXX XX9 984 - Los Angeles
February 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur Avazian, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 20, 2004, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on February 9, 2004. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent contends that he demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear, based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent contends that an employee of his former attorney, Gerald Cobb, Esquire, told him that his next hearing was scheduled for February 19, 2004, rather than the correct date of February 9, 2004. Based on this representation, the respondent did not appear for his hearing on February 9, 2004. With his motion to reopen, the respondent submitted numerous documents, including his declaration, his wife’s declaration, and the declaration of Mr. Cobb, his former attorney. In his declaration, the respondent indicates that he has been in removal proceedings since April 2000, and he has had many court appearances since then. He also states that he does not blame Mr. Cobb himself, and since he has admitted that there was an error in his office regarding the date, he does not intend to file a complaint with the State Bar against Mr. Cobb. In Mr. Cobb’s declaration, he states that his former receptionist erroneously informed the respondent that the date of his next hearing was February 19, 2004. Mr. Cobb also notes that he has very limited experience practicing immigration law and was unaware of what procedure to follow to preserve respondent’s rights and protect his position.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).
 
Precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, provides that while the requirements of Lozada are generally reasonable, they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 n.6 (9th Cir. 2000). The Ninth Circuit determined that “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra. Furthermore, where the face of the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel, there has been substantial compliance with the rule. Id. Finally, the Ninth Circuit has determined that under certain circumstances, the failure to file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities does not defeat an alien’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003) (Court noted that aliens had appeared at all previously scheduled removal hearings; alien had filed an extensive application that presented substantial evidence of eligibility for discretionary relief from removal; and neither he nor his wife had any reason to miss their hearing and subject themselves to an in absentia order).
 
*2 The record in the instant case supports the conclusion that based on the totality of circumstances, the respondent has established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. It appears that due to the actions of a former employee of Mr. Cobb, the respondent was informed of an incorrect date for his next scheduled appearance before the Immigration Court. The respondent submitted his own affidavit and Mr. Cobb’s affidavit. The respondent also explained why he did not file a State Bar complaint against Mr. Cobb. Furthermore, the record of proceedings reflects that the respondent had appeared for hearings in the Los Angeles Immigration Court numerous times between April 2000 and June 2003. Consequently, it would be inconsistent for the respondent to fail to appear for a hearing. The record also reflects the respondent was awaiting the approval of a visa petition filed on his behalf, which apparently has now been approved. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We note that the requirement that a bar complaint be filed is to ensure that collusion does not occur, and that the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is a valid one. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Therefore, as we find that counsel did afford the respondent ineffective assistance and in light of the lack of any bar complaint, we shall forward this matter to the Executive Office for Immigration Review Bar counsel for review and the institution of any disciplinary measures deemed appropriate. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

IN RE: FRANK MORELLI
File: AXX XX0 778 - San Diego
February 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jonathan D. Montag, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The Board on December 21, 2004, denied a motion to reopen filed by the respondent. The respondent on January 4, 2005, filed a motion seeking to have the Board reconsider this order. The motion, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), will be granted.
 
On April 22, 2002, the Board affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision which found the respondent subject to removal under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(i), for having been convicted on March 25, 1999, of a crime of domestic violence. More than 2 years later, on July 23, 2004, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen.
 
The respondent claimed in his motion to reopen that the motions deadline should be tolled because he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney, who represented him until 2004 before the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. The Immigration Judge on June 5, 2000, determined that the respondent was ineligible for cancellation of removal, for permanent residents, under section 240A(a) of the Act, because he had not been lawfully admitted for permanent residence for 5 years, having been adjusted to that status on June 18, 1996. See section 240A(a)(1) of the Act. The respondent argued in the motion to reopen that former counsel failed to file a motion to remand on or after June 18, 2001, when his eligibility for relief “ripened”, Mot. to Reopen, at 3. In our December 21, 2004, decision, we determined that the respondent was nonetheless ineligible for cancellation of removal, because his continuous residence ended on January 14, 1999, the day that he committed a crime involving moral turpitude, and prior counsel committed no error by not pursuing relief for the respondent. Upon the respondent’s pending motion, we have reconsidered this decision. At the time the respondent committed his crime, he had already accrued the 7 years of continuous residence that is required under section 240A(a)(2) of the Act, as he presents evidence that he was admitted to the United States in 1989 as a nonimmigrant, and the DHS does not dispute this.
 
The respondent has complied with Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Former counsel admits he erred by not filing a timely motion to reopen seeking cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Our order of December 21, 2004, is rescinded.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The case is remanded to the immigration court to provide the respondent the opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal.

2005 WL 698362 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS MONJE PEREZ A.K.A. JESUS PEREZ
File: AXX XX7 684 - Los Angeles
February 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed. We concur with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent has failed to demonstrate plausible grounds for relief. Specifically, the primary relief the respondent seeks is reopening of his proceedings after an in absentia order of removal was entered. The request for reopening is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (both with respect to the reason for the respondent’s failure to appear and with respect to a failure to perfect an earlier appeal with this Board). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The ultimate relief sought is a waiver under section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
The record reflects that on June 15, 1999, the respondent was served with Notice to Appear alleging that he was removable from the United States pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A). On November 3, 1999, the respondent was ordered removed in absentia when he failed to appear at his scheduled removal hearing. The record reflects that the respondent was provided proper notice of the hearing and that he had been personally informed of the date of the hearing and of the consequences of not appearing on that date.
 
Neither the respondent, who is now pro se, or any of his former attorneys, has ever presented a persuasive factual or legal argument that proceedings should be reopened. Assuming arguendo that the respondent established that his attorney actually miscalendared the date of the November, 1999 hearing, as claimed, the respondent still has not established that the attorney’s conduct prejudiced the respondent to a sufficient degree to justify reopening of proceedings. See Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984). The respondent, not his attorney, was primarily responsible for attending his removal hearing. The respondent had been personally notified of the date of the hearing and the consequences of failing to appear. The alleged mistake on the part of the respondent’s attorney did not prevent the respondent from complying with the minimal responsibility of attending his removal hearing. Consequently, the respondent has not demonstrated a plausible basis for relief. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (detailing the necessary steps for making a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and discussing the basis for a finding that a respondent was prejudiced by ineffective assistance).
 
*2 We note that recent regulatory changes affecting the eligibility for section 212(c) relief for certain aliens, on which the respondent partially relies in his assertion that proceedings in this case should be reopened, do not contain any relevant change in the requirements for obtaining reopening of proceedings after the entry of an in absentia order of removal. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3 (2004).
 
In sum, there is no basis on which to sustain the respondent’s appeal. In light of the foregoing, we need not reach the other issues raised on appeal by the respondent and the DHS.
 
The respondent was correctly found removable from the United States and he has demonstrated no basis on which his request to reopen proceedings should be granted. Consequently, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 698330 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANUEL MORALES SANGALANG
File: AXX XX3 622 - Orlando
February 16, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Neil A. Weinrib, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on February 14, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia removal order entered on April 12, 2001. We found that the respondent had not shown the exceptional circumstances required for reopening his case. The matter is now before us pursuant to the October 31, 2003, decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit. The court remanded the case for the Board to consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
In light of the court’s decision, and upon further review of the record, the removal proceedings will be reopened. The respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Lozada. See generally Dakane v. U.S. Attorney General, 371 F.3d 771 (11th Cir. 2004). Further, it appears that the respondent did not appear for his hearing because he was “blatantly misled” by his counsel that he did not need to appear. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 474 (BIA 1996). The respondent also appears to be eligible to apply to adjust his status, so he has every reason to appear for his removal hearing.
 
Accordingly, the decision of the Board dated February 14, 2002, is vacated, the removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. The respondent’s unopposed motion to change the venue of his case from Orlando, Florida, to New York, New York, is hereby granted.

2005 WL 698342 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER MARAVILLA-MARAVILLA CLAUDIA LOPEZ-SANCHEZ
Files: AXX XX1 613 - San Francisco
AXX XX1 614
February 16, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Paul M. Kaplan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents in this case are husband and wife, both natives and citizens of Mexico. Their case was previously before the Board on April 24, 2002, when we summarily affirmed, without opinion, the results of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying their applications for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge found that the respondents had not shown that they satisfied the 10 years continuous physical presence requirement for nonimmigrant cancellation of removal, and had not shown that their citizen children, or the male respondent’s lawful permanent resident father, would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if the respondents were removed to Mexico. On January 9, 2003, the Board denied the respondents’ motion to reopen based on their claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The matter is now before us pursuant to the August 19, 2004, decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The court found that the Board did not adequately or properly consider the respondents’ claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. It remanded the case for the Board to further consider whether adequate counsel would have acted differently in this case, and, if so, whether the respondents were prejudiced by counsel’s inadequate performance.
 
In light of the court’s decision, and upon further review of the record, we will grant the respondents’ motion to reopen and will remand the case to the Immigration Judge. A review of the transcript supports the respondents’ claim that their prior counsel did not adequately prepare them to fully present their cancellation applications, on either the issue of physical presence, or hardship. Further, the brief filed by former counsel on appeal is cursory, at best, and incorrectly includes boilerplate language regarding the hardship standard for suspension of deportation, not cancellation of removal. In addition, as noted by the respondents, prior counsel never brought to the Board’s attention the fact that the male respondent’s mother became a lawful permanent resident during the pendency of the appeal.
 
We have also noted the Immigration Judge’s complaints at the hearing regarding counsel’s performance in this and other cases, as well as the fact that former counsel has now been disbarred. Given all these considerations, we find that the respondents’ former counsel provided inadequate representation at their hearing, and in representing them in their appeal to this Board. We further find that counsel’s inadequate performance “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 900 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
*2 Finally, we note that the last hearing in this case was held nearly 6 years ago. The respondents’ citizen children are now 10 and 9 years old. The male respondent’s parents are apparently partly dependent upon the respondents for their support. Under all these circumstances, we find that the respondents should be provided a further opportunity to establish that they are eligible for and deserving of cancellation of removal. See generally Matter of Recinas, 23 I&N Dec. 467 (BIA 2002).
 
Accordingly, the decisions of the Board in this case dated April 24, 2002, and January 9, 2003, are vacated, the removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for a further hearing.
  
<Signature>

2005 WL 698337 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER BARRON-DIAZ
File: A92 993 112 - San Diego
February 14, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph C. Barrera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J. Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated September 9, 2003, in which the Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal and denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent has moved for a remand to the Immigration Judge for a new hearing. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
The issue on appeal involves whether the respondent, who is a lawful permanent resident, was properly found subject to removal. The respondent’s appeal raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The record establishes that on May 1, 2001, the first Immigration Judge to whom this case was assigned ordered the respondent’s former counsel, Attorney McCandless, to submit a pre-hearing statement setting out any objections to the admission of the Form I-213, Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien, pertaining to the respondent, which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) sought to submit into evidence (Exhs. 2, 6). That attorney failed to file any such statement during the time permitted. Three months after the deadline for the statement, the Immigration Judge issued an order finding that any objections to the admission of the Form I-213 were waived (Exh. 6). The Immigration Judge further found that the Form I-213 contained a sufficient factual basis to establish allegations regarding alienage and was clear, cogent, and convincing evidence of the alien-smuggling charge in the respondent’s Notice to Appear (Exhs. 1, 6). The Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged and set a hearing date for the respondent to apply for relief from removal (Exh. 6).
 
The respondent’s former counsel failed to appear for the hearing. The respondent arrived in the courtroom after the Immigration Judge had issued an order of removal in absentia. Thereafter proceedings were reopened pursuant to a motion to reopen, which was filed by respondent’s former counsel.
 
After proceedings were reopened, the case was assigned to a new Immigration Judge. At the February 25, 2003, hearing, the respondent’s former counsel did not appear but instead sent substitute counsel, Attorney Miller. The respondent agreed to be represented by Attorney Miller as well as by Attorney McCandless (Tr. at 13). During the continued hearing, the Immigration Judge first stated that the Form I-213 was unclear about whether the respondent admitted knowing the passenger he carried in his car was illegal before the car was stopped at the Mexican border and that this should be considered by the respondent when deciding whether to concede the charge of removability, which was for alien smuggling (Tr. at 21-22, 25). At the next hearing date, the Immigration Judge berated Attorney Miller for suggesting that the charge of removability would be contested (Tr. at 28-29). The Immigration Judge stated that removability had been established by the prior Immigration Judge and indicated that Attorney Miller was wasting the court’s time (Tr. at 29-31). The Immigration Judge appeared to have no recollection whatsoever of his prior instruction concerning this matter. Attorney Miller telephoned the office of Attorney McCandless and then advised the Immigration Judge that the charge of removability would not be challenged (Tr. at 31-35). A hearing was set to consider the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. Neither Attorney McCandless nor Attorney Miller appeared at that hearing, and the Immigration Judge proceeded without them (Tr. at 44-47). After a hearing on the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, the application was denied.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent is represented by new counsel. The respondent makes no specific challenges to the Immigration Judge’s denial of cancellation of removal. He states only that he was prevented from presenting his case when neither of his attorneys appeared for his hearing. The respondent indicates that after his attorneys did not appear, he requested the Immigration Judge to grant a continuance. However, no such request is reflected by the record. On appeal, the respondent seeks a remand to allow him to present his case. With respect to the issue of removability, the respondent states that his former counsel erred in conceding removability and in failing to inform the Immigration Judge of a witness who was willing to testify that the respondent did not know the person he was driving across the Mexican border was an illegal alien.
 
We find that the respondent has not established his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Although we do not discount the seriousness of the respondent’s attorneys’ failure to appear at the hearing, we nevertheless do not find that the respondent has established he was ultimately prejudiced by his attorneys’ actions or inactions. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640.1
 
The Form I-213 in the record states that the respondent advised officials he knew the person he was transporting did not present valid documents at the border (Exh. 2). Furthermore, the alien-smuggling incident at issue occurred after the respondent had twice before been apprehended while transporting aliens, who lacked valid documents, across the Mexican border (Tr. at 75-77). The respondent admitted at his hearing that he knew one of these aliens was attempting to enter the United States illegally (Tr. at 77). On appeal, the respondent does not address Attorney McCandless’s failure to file a challenge to the admission of the Form I-213. Moreover, the respondent does not indicate what such a challenge would allege. The respondent’s appeal brief states that the respondent’s attorneys failed to advise the Immigration Judge of a witness who could testify the respondent did not know the alien he was transporting lacked valid entry documents. However, the respondent did not submit an affidavit stating that he advised his attorneys about this potential witness. The respondent has not submitted any other evidence with respect to his challenge to the finding of removability. We find no error in the conclusion that the respondent is subject to removal as charged. Therefore, we do not find the outcome of the respondent’s case would have been different but for his attorneys’ failure to provide appropriate legal services. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 527 n.12 (9th Cir. 2000) (noting that due process challenges to deportation proceedings require a showing of prejudice). Finally, as previously noted, the respondent does not specifically allege any error in the denial of cancellation of removal.
 
*3 For these reasons, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.

See Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO FLOREZ DELGADILLO
File: AXX XX2 035 - San Francisco
February 11, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank P. Sprouls, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace H. Cheung,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 In an order entered on July 30, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has, through counsel, filed a motion to reopen, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security. The motion will be denied.
 
The basis for the motion, in its essence, is that counsel himself “failed utterly” in filing a petition for review of our order, and that the case should be reopened to reissue our order with a new date, to permit its review by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit.
 
In the first instance, the motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the second, the respondent has not shown sufficient compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2004). While the motion is associated with a photocopy of Mr. Sprouls’ alleged letter to his state bar, admitting “ineffective assistance of counsel,” there is no evidence that the letter was actually filed or that the respondent actually submitted a formal complaint against him. Although there is a photocopy of an attorney-client contract, dated August 23, 2004, with the motion, it is unclear when the respondent actually contacted Mr. Sprouls regarding representation. If the respondent had waited until after the expiration of the voluntary departure period, he would be ineligible for cancellation in any event.
 
Additionally, the respondent is utterly silent on this matter. The respondent has not explained when he contacted Mr. Sprouls; what his understanding was of the actions Mr. Sprouls was to take on his behalf; what efforts he himself made in the preparation of his petition, if any; what the level and frequency of communication was between Mr. Sprouls and him regarding any legal representation; and what actions he took in regard to filing a complaint with Mr. Sprouls’ state bar. At a minimum, we would expect an affidavit from the respondent supporting the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Given the dearth of facts in, and associated with, the motion, we do not think it a rigid application of our holding in Matter of Lozada, supra, to deny this motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
