Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BORIS OSWALDO MEDINA-HERRERA
File: AXX XX1 428 - New Orleans
February 10, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul S. Zoltan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Remained longer than permitted
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) to reconsider our decision dated November 18, 2004. We will deny the motion.
 
On October 30, 1990, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent deported when he failed to appear at a scheduled deportation hearing. See former section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b). On December 17, 1999, the Immigration Judge denied a motion wherein the respondent requested reopening to enable him to apply for benefits under the NACARA. On February 5, 2004, the respondent filed a motion wherein he requested reopening and reconsideration of the December 17, 1999, decision. In that motion, the respondent argued that he had been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. On February 23, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen and reconsider based on his finding that motion was filed in an untimely manner. The respondent filed a timely appeal from the February 23, 2004, decision, and we dismissed the appeal on November 18, 2004. This timely motion to reconsider followed. The respondent now argues that we erred as a matter of law in dismissing the appeal. For the following reasons, we disagree.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent asserts that we improperly declined to consider the issue of whether the respondent received adequate notice of the October 30, 1990, hearing which was conducted in his absence. The record reflects, however, that this issue was not considered or ruled upon by the Immigration Judge. Indeed, it is undisputed that the respondent had not raised the issue at any time before the Immigration Judge. We stress that it is the function of this Board, as an appellate body, to review a record as it existed before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1984). The issue of the adequacy of notice was not, therefore, properly before the Board on appeal. See Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196 n.4 (BIA 1980). We thus find no error in our decision declining to consider the “notice” issue in our decision dismissing the appeal.
 
The respondent further asserts that we erred in concluding that he was not entitled to toll the deadline for the motion to reopen and reconsider to accommodate his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). Specifically, he asserts that we engaged in “impermissible factfinding” when we determined that the respondent failed to establish that he merits equitable tolling because he failed to act with due diligence in exercising his rights. In response to the respondent’s assertion, we emphasize that our decision with regard to equitable tolling was based on our analysis of the facts which were before the Immigration Judge at the time of the February 23, 2004, decision. In light of those facts, we disagree with the assertion, which the respondent has put forth in the instant motion, that a “factfinder might well find that a ‘reasonable person’ in the Respondent’s situation … would not have heard alarm bells when he received no immediate word on his NACARA motion to reopen.” We stress that a period of more than 4 years had passed between the Immigration Judge’s December 17, 1999, decision and the respondent’s February 5, 2004, motion to reopen and reconsider. We note that in the “bar complaint,” which the respondent submitted to the Immigration Judge in support of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent stated that “it seemed odd” that he had not received any correspondence from his attorney after he contacted the attorney’s office on or about September 11, 1998. He also stated in the complaint, however, that he “decided not to contact (his former attorney) for various reasons, primarily because of the unprofessional and disrespectful manner in which she made (him) feel.” The fact that the respondent chose to eschew communication with his attorney, even though he felt that “it seemed odd” that the attorney had not communicated with him since 1998, leads us to stand by our conclusion that he failed to act with due diligence in this case.
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, we will not alter our November 18, 2004, decision. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

2005 WL 698551 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO ZAMORA MARTINEZ
File: AXX XX1 470 - Los Angeles
February 10, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elsa Martinez, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated February 9, 2004. The respondent contends that his former representative afforded him ineffective assistance of counsel as he failed to file an appeal brief.
 
The respondent reported in his motion that he did not learn of his former attorney’s failure to file a brief on appeal until he consulted with his current attorney. The record contains an August 2004 letter from the respondent’s new representative seeking review of the record of proceedings, and his motion was filed within two months of that date. In light of this evidence, we find that the respondent exercised diligence in submitting the pending motion, and the deadline for such motions should be tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent has submitted evidence suggesting that he complied with the requirements for a motion contending ineffective assistance of counsel by detailing the agreement he had with his former counsel, notifying him of the allegations against him, and filing a complaint with the appropriate bar authority. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We agree that the respondent received ineffective assistance from his former counsel. However, the respondent must also demonstrate that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that alien is prejudiced if attorney’s conduct may have affected the outcome of the proceeding); Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
However, the respondent has not provided any evidence with the motion which indicates that the outcome of his appeal might have been different had a brief been filed. The Board did not summarily dismiss his appeal for failure to file a brief, but summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s adverse findings. The respondent, other than generally asserting that he meets the requisite criteria for a grant of cancellation of removal, has not addressed these negative findings with any specificity. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was prejudiced by his former attorney in these proceedings, and reopening is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: IOANNIS S. ASLANIS

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark T. Kenmore, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Denise C. Hochul
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(9)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Previously ordered removed or departed while order of removal was outstanding
 
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver, reopening
 
The respondent appeals an Immigration Judge’s October 3, 2003, decision finding him ineligible for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed). The respondent also requests that the removal proceedings be reopened and that the case be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. We will deny the motion to reopen and dismiss the appeal.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent is ineligible for a section 212(c) waiver because he is no longer a lawful permanent resident after self-deporting himself from the United States in 1997 following an Immigration Judge’s November 18, 1991, decision ordering him deported to Greece (Tr. at 31). See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (the Board may adopt or affirm an Immigration Judge’s decision, in whole or in part); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1.1(p), 241.7, 1003.4, 1003.39; Matter of Lok, 18 I&N Dec. 101, 105 (BIA 1981) (the lawful permanent resident status of an alien ends with the entry of a final administrative order of deportation); see also 69 Fed. Reg. 57826, 57834 (September 28, 2004) (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)) (aliens issued a final order of deportation, who then illegally return to the United States, are not eligible to file a special motion to seek section 212(c) relief).
 
*2 We reject the respondent’s contention that his due process rights were violated because he was not notified of the consequences of departing the United States. The regulations provided sufficient notice to his counsel and to him of the consequences of his leaving the United States during the pendency of his appeal with the Board. The case that he cites in his brief, Matter of Brown, 18 I&N Dec. 324 (BIA 1982), is inapposite to this case because it concerned the departure of the alien during deportation proceedings. The respondent is also ineligible for cancellation of removal because he is not a lawful permanent resident, and because he has been convicted of a drug offense. See sections 240A(a), 240A(b)(1)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(a), 1229b(b)(1)(C).
 
We deny the motion to reopen and remand the case based upon ineffective assistance of counsel because it is clearly untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of the final administrative order). Furthermore, assuming, without deciding, that the respondent complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozado, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), he has not demonstrated that he exercised due diligence to justify the equitably tolling of the motions deadlines because there is no evidence that he maintained contact with the attorney that filed the appeal with the Board (Tr. at 30-31). See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2nd Cir. 2000) (the alien must demonstrate that he has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period he seeks to toll). Accordingly, we enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied and the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 698537 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULU, THEOPHILUS TEAH
File: AXX-XX4-500 - Newark
February 9, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fernandez, Regis, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated March 31, 2003. The respondent’s untimely motion to reopen is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Although the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), he has failed to prove any prejudice resulting from his former counsel’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The motion is denied. The respondent’s documentation establishes that his former attorney, J.D. Larosiliere, absconded from New Jersey and abandoned his clients in February 2002. See Motion to Reopen, Attachment B at 12. At the time, the respondent’s appeal was pending before this Board. The record reveals that Mr. Montilus, an associate of Mr. Larosiliere, represented the respondent before the Immigration Judge. On June 19, 2001, Mr. Larosiliere filed an appeal brief on behalf of the respondent. On March 31, 2003, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent claims that he first discovered that his appeal was dismissed in April 2004, when he was arrested. Even if we accept the respondent’s claim that he first learned of our order a year after it was issued, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by Mr. Larosiliere’s misconduct during the course of these deportation proceedings. The respondent cites no misconduct that occurred before the Immigration Judge. Nor does he cite any error in the appeal brief that was filed with this Board. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2005 WL 698540 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEJANDRO HERNANDEZ NUNEZ
File: AXX XX3 352 - Los Angeles
February 9, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ta-Yu Yang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Debra H. Workeneh
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear at the hearing on March 29, 2004. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that he never received the notice of hearing from the Immigration Court or his attorney. He contends that he was working in Iowa at the time of his hearing, and that he has the right to work outside of the immigration district in which his case was pending.
 
We find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was served personally on the respondent on May 7, 1998. The hearing notice was mailed to the respondent’s attorney of record on July 10, 2003, with a hearing scheduled for March 29, 2004. As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Furthermore, it appears that the respondent failed to stay in contact with his attorney to ascertain his hearing date, or to provide new contact information for himself while he was working in Iowa. The respondent also did not submit an affidavit in support of his motion to reopen. The respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that based upon the evidence of record, the respondent received notice of the hearing in accordance with the Act, and he has not met the burden of demonstrating lack of notice under the statute. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 698588 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN RAMOS-ORTIZ
Files: A95 208 756 - Kansas City
February 9, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se1
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paula V. Davis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 16, 2004, in which she denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on January 20, 2004. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he did not receive notice from anyone that his case was scheduled for a hearing on January 20, 2004, and that he only received notice from the court and his attorney that the hearing was scheduled for February 3, 2004. The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge incorrectly held that if the motion to reopen was based on exceptional circumstances, his motion would be time barred because it was filed more than 90 days after entry of the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
Initially, we agree with the Immigration Judge that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that on September 8, 2003, a new notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s attorney of record, indicating that the respondent’s hearing had been rescheduled to January 20, 2004. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 10013.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a). The Immigration Judge noted that the new notice of hearing was never returned as undeliverable and it was mailed over four months before the new hearing date. See I.J. Dec. at 2. Furthermore, the respondent’s former attorney of record did not submit an affidavit with the motion to reopen attesting to his failure to receive the new notice of hearing. The motion to reopen contains a purported declaration of the respondent, but it is signed by the respondent’s former attorney of record.
 
We agree with the respondent that the Immigration Judge incorrectly determined that if the respondent’s motion to reopen was based on exceptional circumstances, his motion was time barred as it was filed more than 90 days after the in absentia order of removal. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(h)(4)(ii), an order of removal entered in absentia pursuant to section 240(h)(5) of the Act may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if based on exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. A review of the record reflects that the Immigration Judge’s order of removal was entered in absentia on January 20, 2004. The respondent filed his motion to reopen on May 24, 2004. Therefore the respondent filed his motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the order of removal, as required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. The respondent claims that he has established an exceptional circumstance based on his failure to receive notice of the rescheduled hearing. However, as discussed above, notice of the new hearing was sent to the respondent’s former attorney of record. See Matter of Barocio, supra. The respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, nor has he attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 698530 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE AGUILAR-LOPEZ
File: AXX XX1 777 - San Francisco
February 8, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Kahn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret Curry
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on March 19, 1991, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of his application for suspension of deportation and denial of a request for a continuance. On November 29, 2004, the respondent submitted a motion to reconsider and reopen proceedings, asserting that the Immigration Judge and this Board erred in our prior decisions, his former counsel afforded him ineffective assistance of counsel, and contending that he is now eligible for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion is untimely, and is denied.
 
We do not find that the respondent’s situation falls within the Ninth Circuit’s equitable tolling policy for aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). The Ninth Circuit has stated that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 897.
 
In the present case, the respondent admits in his declaration that he was aware that an appeal had been filed on his behalf, stating that he was in contact with his former representative a year after the appeal was filed, which would have been in 1987. The respondent alleges that he was provided with ineffective assistance because his former counsel failed to inform him of our March 1991, decision. However, the respondent has not specified what efforts he made to remain in contact with his counsel during the more than 4-year period his case was on appeal, and the more than thirteen years since the Board issued its adverse decision. The respondent admittedly spoke with another attorney in, apparently, 1989, and learned that his former counsel had closed her office. Yet he apparently took no further action to either contact his former representative, or otherwise ascertain the status of his appeal. Consequently, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that he acted diligently in this case.
 
We also find that the respondent has not demonstrated either that his attorney did, in fact, afford him ineffective assistance, or that he suffered any prejudice arising from former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness. The respondent’s disagreement with his former counsel’s tactical decisions regarding admitting the factual allegations and conceding removability do not, without more, support a finding of ineffective assistance. Moreover, the record supports these actions, which includes a record of deportable alien and a record of conviction which indicate that the respondent entered the United States in December 1979, and that they terminated his probation in March 1986 with a 180-day sentence imposed to be served consecutively with another sentence, with evidence that he was released in November 1986 (Exhs. 2, 3). Thus evidence then of record supported the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent entered the United States in December 1979, which contradicts the respondent’s uncorroborated assertion that he entered prior to that time. Moreover, while the respondent contests the Immigration Judge’s, and this Board’s, finding that he was incarcerated more than 180 days he has not reported what term he in fact served, and, as noted, the record supports the factual findings in this regard. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish prejudice as a result of his former counsel’s assistance, or that the same was ineffective. Iturribarria, 321 F.3d at 899-900 (in order to show prejudice, a petitioner must establish his counsel’s performance “was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”).
 
*2 Finally, even if the respondent’s motion were not otherwise barred, we note that a motion to reopen for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all the supporting documentation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). In the present case, the respondent has not submitted proof of an approved visa petition. In fact, the respondent has not provided evidence that the immigrant petition has been filed on his behalf. Nor has he provided evidence that he is married or that his wife is a United States citizen. In addition, he has not supported the motion with an application to adjust his status. A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the respondent establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Here we find that the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility to adjust his status.
 
Therefore, for all of the above reasons, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: MENDEZ, ANA LILIA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Moreno, Leticia T.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 07, 2003. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to establish eligibility for cancellation of removal for not showing the requisite degree of hardship to the respondent’s qualifying relatives. The decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed for the reasons provided therein. On appeal, the respondent argued that the respondent was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and did not receive a fair hearing. According to the respondent, the respondent’s former attorney did not adequately prepare for the hearing, and improperly withdrew as counsel prior thereto. Notwithstanding, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Namely, there is no indication that a complaint was filed against former counsel, and whether he was given an opportunity to reply to the allegations. In addition, the respondent has not provided any evidence of hardship that was not presented or considered by the Immigration Judge. In the absence thereof, we see no reason to remand the record for a new hearing. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 698525 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NADER H. CHMAISSANI
File: AXX XX4 955 - Detroit
February 7, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tamara French, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rosario S. Shoudy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on July 19, 2004, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be granted, and proceedings will be reopened.
 
In his motion, the respondent argues that former counsel failed to adequately prepare him for trial, failed to file an appeal brief which resulted in the summary dismissal of his appeal, and failed to timely notify him of the Board’s decision, which caused him to unwittingly overstay the period of voluntary departure. As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, by submitting an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, evidence that a grievance has been filed, and proof that prior counsel has been notified of the allegations. However, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by his counsel’s performance at the hearing or by the failure to file an appeal brief. See id., at 638 (holding that an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must show that he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance). Although he claims that his former attorney only met with him once before the hearing, he has not specified what evidence or testimony his attorney failed to elicit or adduce. Current counsel even concedes that she “is not in a position to allege prejudice as to the substance of the merits hearing” (Motion, at 6). Likewise, the respondent’s bare assertion that he was prejudiced by the failure to file an appeal brief is insufficient to demonstrate the requisite prejudice. The fact that prior counsel did not submit a brief does not, in itself, amount to a deprivation of due process. Id.; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (finding that the attorney’s failure to file an appeal brief was not per se prejudicial where the respondent made no showing in his motion that the Immigration Judge erred in denying relief).
 
However, the respondent has demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s failure to inform him of the Board’s decision and voluntary departure date. By overstaying the period of voluntary departure, the respondent’s ability to adjust his status based upon his impending marriage to his United States citizen fiancee could be seriously impeded. See section 242B(e)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(e)(2)(1995) (stating that any alien allowed to depart voluntarily who remains in the United States after the scheduled date of departure, other than because of exceptional circumstances, shall not be eligible for certain forms of relief, including adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, for a period of 5 years after the scheduled date of departure).
 
*2 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) argues that the respondent has failed to establish statutory eligibility for voluntary departure. Specifically, it asserts that the respondent is “clearly unwilling to depart” due to his recent engagement to a United States citizen, and that the respondent lacks a valid travel document. However, with his motion the respondent has submitted a copy of his current passport, along with an affidavit attesting to his willingness to depart voluntarily. Thus, as we find that the respondent established that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to notify the respondent of his voluntary departure date, we will reopen proceedings to reinstate the period of voluntary departure.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and in accordance with our decision in Matter of Chouliaris, 16 I&N Dec. 168 (BIA 1977), the respondent is permitted to depart from the United States voluntarily within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security; and in the event of failure so to depart, the respondent shall be deported as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.

2005 WL 698523 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEVON AUGUSTUS PITTER
File: AXX XX8 135 - Baltimore
February 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Goren, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed. The transcript of proceedings shows that the respondent, by counsel, conceded that he was subject to removal as an aggravated felon, having been convicted of a sexual offense involving a minor, and waived his right to appeal (Tr. at 5, 7). The Immigration Judge noted in his August 17, 2004, decision that the respondent had waived appeal. On September 13, 2004, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal). The respondent on the Notice of Appeal argues that his attorney did not adequately explain his options to him, and this led to his accepting the waiver. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). However, despite making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making such a claim. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Stewart v. INS, 181 F.3d 587, 596 n. 9 (4th Cir. 1999).
 
In any event, the respondent’s brief states that he is seeking to have his conviction overturned through a motion in criminal court. However, such efforts to collaterally attack his conviction do not affect the respondent’s present removability. See generally Matter of Khalik, 17 I&N Dec. 518, 519 (BIA 1980); see also Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750, 752 (BIA 1993); Marino v. INS, 537 F.3d 686, 691-92 (2d Cir. 1976).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

2005 WL 698519 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOISES MORALES-GABRIEL
File: AXX XX5 820 - Portland, Oregon
February 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Keith Jordan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marty D. Ryan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of Status
 
The respondent, a male native and citizen of Mexico, appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 8, 2003. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s July 8, 2003, decision, pretermitting the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent’s visa petition was not filed on or before April 30, 2001, as prescribed by section 245(i) of the Act. See 245(i)(1)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i)(1)(B)(i). Moreover, there is no evidence on the record that the respondent has an approved visa petition reflecting an immediately available visa. Accordingly, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is statutorily ineligible to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act.
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that a visa petition was not filed on or before April 30, 2001, due to the erroneous advice of prior counsel. The respondent claims that, as such, the Board should deem his visa petition as timely filed. Without regard to whether the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel in the present case, we find the respondent’s argument that he is entitled to such a remedy unavailing. The respondent’s reliance on Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), to support his contention is inapposite. Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, involved an alien whose counsel failed to timely file a suspension of deportation application before the deadline set by the Immigration Judge. Id. at 522. The present case, however, involves the respondent’s failure to file a visa petition application with the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) on or before April 30, 2001, a fixed statutory cut-off deadline set by Congress. To be “grandfathered” pursuant to section 245(i) of the Act, the respondent’s visa petition needed to be submitted on or before the requisite cut-off date, and be accepted as “properly filed” and “approvable when filed” by the DHS. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1245.10(a)(1)(i)(A), (2)(i), (3). The respondent urges the Board to constructively deem his visa petition as properly filed before the April 30, 2001, deadline. Because the DHS has sole jurisdiction in determining the sufficiency of visa petitions submitted pursuant to section 245(i) of the Act, we find that neither the Board nor an Immigration Judge has the jurisdiction to create the equitable remedy the respondent seeks. In view of the foregoing, the following orders shall be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 698496 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZAHIDUL HOQUE CHOWDHURY LOVELY ZAHID CHOWDHURY RUSLAT ZAHID CHOWDHURY FARRUBA ZAHID CHOWDHURY
Files: AXX XX6 598 - New York
AXX XX6 599
AXX XX6 601
AXX XX6 602
February 3, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Ruchi Thaker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard VanVeldhuisen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents have filed a motion to reconsider, in which they seek reconsideration of the Board’s August 20, 2004, decision. In our August 20, 2004, decision we denied a motion to reopen as untimely under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
In our prior order, we considered the respondents’ claim that prior counsel was incompetent, because prior counsel failed to inform them of the Board’s prior decision so they could file a timely motion to reopen. We determined that there was no basis for equitable tolling of the motions deadline, see Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 127, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000), because the motion to reopen was filed almost a year late, and the respondents did not say when they learned of the Board’s prior order. We also found that the respondents had failed to comply with Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondents now say that they learned of the Board’s March 21, 2003, decision on December 16, 2003 (Motion to Reconsider, at p. 6; Lead Respondent’s Affidavit), and claim that they have complied with Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra. The motion to reopen was not filed, however, until April 23, 2004, another 129 days after the respondents claim that they learned of our March 21, 2003, order. That the respondents failed to find counsel to advise them to file a motion to reopen more promptly, that was initially in compliance with the procedural requirements of Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, is not a basis for tolling the motions deadline for almost a year.
 
We also note that an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, supra; Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 110 (2d Cir. 1993). The Department of Labor issued an approved labor certification concerning the lead respondent on June 24, 2003, and the employment-based visa filed on the respondent’s behalf was approved on November 9, 2004 - both dates occurred after the time for filing a timely motion to reopen had expired. Therefore, no prejudice is shown in prior counsel allegedly not informing the respondent of the deadline for filing a motion to reopen.
 
*2 We could only reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), which the respondents urge us to do. We take this action only in “exceptional situations.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002). We do not find that this is such an exceptional situation.
 
The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2005 WL 649054 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAUL REYNALDO RIVERA RIVAS
File: AXX XX9 224 - Los Angeles
February 2, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shan Potts, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves to reopen proceedings that concluded with this Board’s decision dated February 2, 1999. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent argues that he was not provided proper notice of the hearing that resulted in his in absentia order of removal. The respondent concedes that notice of hearing was mailed and actually received by his attorney of record. Service on a respondent’s attorney or representative of record constitutes proper service. 8 C.F.R. § 292.5.
 
The respondent alternatively argues that he was provided ineffective assistance of counsel because his former attorney of record did not effectively communicate the notice of hearing to the respondent. We have held that an alien must support a claim of ineffective assistance by a detailed affidavit, inform his previous attorney of the allegations of misconduct and provide the attorney with an opportunity to respond, and state whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the attorney’s alleged violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), afff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Here, the applicant has not submitted any evidence to support his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has submitted an unsworn statement that fails to provide a sufficient factual basis for this Board to conclude that he was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, the respondent has failed to submit any evidence that he informed his former counsel of the complaint, and the “complaint” the respondent contends was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority is an untranslated document that appears to be a form containing no statement describing the alleged misconduct. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.33 (regarding the submission of untranslated documents). See also, Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (Courts have held in similar situations that an alien was not required to fulfill procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, inasmuch as the record indicated a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance). We find that the respondent has failed to submit sufficient evidence to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied.

2005 WL 649048 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEVKI NANDAN BALI
File: A78 642 666 - Phoenix
February 2, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric G. Bjotvedt, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Arthur S. Raznick
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed out of time. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on June 22, 2004. The motion to reopen was filed late, on October 25, 2004. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposses the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
The respondent argues that the deadline for filing his motion to reopen should be tolled due to alleged ineffective assistance of 2 former attorneys. He argues that the attorney who represented him at the removal hearing failed to request on his behalf voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). The respondent further argues that the attorney who represented him on appeal did not claim that ineffective assistance of counsel caused him to forgo voluntary departure, and he consulted current counsel in September, 2004. Guided by Ninth Circuit precedent, we find that the respondent exercised diligence in submitting the pending motion, and the deadline for such motion should be tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir.2003) (court found that to the extent that equitable tolling applies, based on actions of prior counsel, limitations period begins when respondent meets with new counsel).
 
As noted, the respondent claims that his former representatives provided ineffective assistance because he failed to apply for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62; (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988). In his affidavit, the respondent states that his first attorney, Robynn Mocek, agreed to help apply for any benefits that would avoid a deportation order. The respondent states that Mocek did not discuss the availability of voluntary departure. At the removal hearing, Mocek did not request voluntary departure, or discuss the issue. The DHS argues that it is “speculative” whether the respondent would have been granted such relief, had it been pursued at the removal hearing. However, the respondent appears to have met the basic requirements for such relief, and provides an affidavit attesting to as much. We note that voluntary departure is a form of relief that is regularly pursued and frequently granted, and there appears to be no reason why it was not sought in this case. On remand, the respondent will have an opportunity to pursue an application for voluntary departure.1 The DHS shall have the opportunity to examine and verify the validity of the respondent’s submissions and present evidence.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

We decline to reopen proceedings for the respondent to “explain his inconsistencies”, Respondent’s Mot., at p. 5, which were found to exist but not to merit a finding of incredible testimony.

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994)(noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondents received proper notice of their hearing scheduled for March 23, 2004. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondents contend that they established exceptional circumstances for their failure to appear due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. Specifically, they assert that they had a serious misunderstanding with their former attorney, and that she failed to properly advise the respondents of their rights and responsibilities, including the necessity of their appearance at the hearing scheduled for March 23, 2004. The respondents submitted new evidence with the notice of appeal in support of their motion to reopen. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Furthermore, although the respondents assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, they have not complied with all of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

IN RE: PERSAD, SHIRLEY

February 1, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Schneps, Barry C., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated November 05, 2004. On November 5, 2004, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the decision of the Immigration Judge which denied her motion to reopen. In that decision, the Board addressed the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and found that the respondent had not presented a claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988). The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider that decision. The respondent has not established any error in our November 2004 decision. The merits of her claim related to ineffective counsel have been reargued. We note, however, that statements of counsel are not evidence. The respondent’s counsel argues that, because the respondent was represented by a non-attorney, she is not bound by the requirements of Lozada, supra. While we do not agree with this argument, we note that the respondent has failed to present any evidence related to the alleged ineffectiveness of representation other than a generalized statement that the individual did not advise her of the date of her hearing. We find this is insufficient to establish a claim of ineffective assistance. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2005 WL 1104600 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SINGH, SANTOKH
File: AXX XX0 701- San Francisco
January 27, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Scott, Sandy E.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason Aguilar
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was properly notified of the hearing. The respondent argued that he complied with his obligation to keep the Immigration Court advised of his current address. He indicated that although he was in removal proceedings in San Francisco, California, documents were filed in Atlanta, Georgia, with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service, which contained the respondent’s correct address, and the DHS should have provided that information to the Immigration Court.
 
In Manjiyani v. INS, 324 F.3d 1138 (9th Cir. 2003), an alien was served with an Order to Show Cause in Seattle, Washington, but the charging document was not filed with the Immigration Court until 2 years later. Thereafter, the alien moved to California, and filed an application for adjustment of status with the DHS in Los Angeles, California. Those forms, however, did not indicate that the alien was served with an Order to Show Cause, and may have been in proceedings in Seattle, Washington. Subsequently, the Order to Show Cause was filed with the Immigration Court in Seattle, Washington, and was sent to the alien at his Washington address. An order of deportation was issued when the alien failed to appear for his hearing held in Seattle. The Ninth Circuit subsequently considered whether the alien had properly notified the DHS of his updated address thereby requiring it to notify the Seattle Immigration Court of the alien’s Los Angeles address. The court held, however, that the alien had not properly notified the DHS of a Change of address because the change was not submitted to the DHS office which issued the Order to Show Cause, and the alien did not inform the Los Angeles DHS office that the DHS office in Washington had issued an Order to Show Cause. Consequently, the Ninth Circuit found that the decision to deny reopening was correct.
 
In the instant case, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had the responsibility to maintain a current address with the Immigration Court, and it was not the burden of the DHS to do so. The respondent herein was already in removal proceedings before an Immigration Judge. In addition, there is no indication that the respondent notified the DHS office in Atlanta, Georgia, that he was in proceedings in San Francisco. In this circumstance, DHS was not obligated to inform the Immigration Court of the respondent’s current address. Furthermore, the respondent infers that his former attorney may have been negligent for not submitting the respondent’s current address to the Immigration Court. The respondent has not, however, complied with the procedural requirements for a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 1104603 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIOVANNI GUERRERO SANTOS, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY MARIA VERONICA PALOMA SANTOS, PETITIONER
File: AXX XX1 181 - San Francisco
January 27, 2005
 
IN VISA PETITION REVOCATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Cynthia Scribe, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kelli J. Duehning
Associate Area Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
 
*1 The petitioner filed on September 20, 1995, a visa petition on behalf of her husband, the beneficiary, to accord him immigration benefits as the spouse of a United States citizen under section 201(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1151(b). That petition was approved on July 27, 1999. The then Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS), now abolished and its visa petitions functions transferred to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (CIS), sent the petitioner an undated Notice of Intent to Revoke in which it alleged that the beneficiary was barred under section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c), from receiving a visa because his first marriage was a sham. The petitioner filed a rebuttal to that notice on April 20, 1999. On July 27, 1999, the INS acting district director revoked the petitioner’ previously approved visa. Counsel for the petitioner filed a Notice of Appeal, with a lengthy written appellate statement, on August 13, 1999, but did not accompany the appeal with a completed Notice of Appearance as Attorney.
 
On May 15, 2000, we returned the record of proceedings to the INS after finding that we lacked jurisdiction over the petitioner’s appeal because the appeal was not accompanied by a completed Notice of Appearance as Attorney (Form G-28). On June 13, 2000, the petitioner filed a timely motion to reconsider.¿FN1º In that motion, the petitioner argues that there is no specific regulation governing which Notice of Appearance must be filed with a visa petition appeal, counsel was not advised of the omission of the Notice of Appearance, and the petitioner should have been considered pro se on appeal rather than having her appeal returned without adjudication by the Board. The record of proceedings was not forwarded to the Board so this motion remains pending.
 
On May 10, 2004, the petitioner, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen, alleging that she received ineffective assistance from her prior attorney whose alleged ineffectiveness resulted in her appeal not having been considered on the merits. We note that the arguments raised in her motion regarding the merits of her appeal are the same arguments that were raised by former counsel in the motion to reconsider. The DHS filed a response in opposition to the petitioner’s motion to which the petitioner filed a reply. The Board will reconsider sua sponte our May 15, 2000, decision. The Board’s May 15, 2000, decision will be vacated. The appeal will be dismissed. The June 13, 2000, motion to reconsider will be denied as moot. The May 10, 2004, motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 We find that the circumstances and procedural posture of this case warrant the reconsideration of our May 15, 2000, decision. We will therefore reconsider our decision sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We further find that our May 15, 2000, decision should be vacated and the petitioner’s appeal reinstated.¿FN2º As we are reconsidering our decision on our own authority, the petitioner’s June 13, 2000, motion to reconsider will be denied as moot.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the INS acting district director erred in revoking her previously approved petition because substantial and probative evidence does not support a finding that the beneficiary’s first marriage was a sham. She contends that the characterizations of the beneficiary’s first wife’s statements that she never lived with the beneficiary and had been paid to marry him are inaccurate. She asserts that there is no literal confession from the beneficiary’s first wife in the record and that the beneficiary’s first wife’s statements regarding receiving $3,000 from the beneficiary and being promised an additional $3,000 does not explicitly indicate that the money was provided so that the beneficiary could obtain legal residency and could have been a loan or a gift. She notes that the beneficiary was not questioned about the money that he provided to his first wife. In addition, the petitioner argues that the beneficiary’s first wife’s statements were obtained only after she twice denied having been paid to marry the beneficiary and then having been threatened with arrest. She asserts that it can be inferred from the record that the beneficiary’s first wife may have felt coerced into stating that the beneficiary gave her money.
 
We find that substantial and probative evidence supports the revocation of the petitioner’s previously approved visa petition and subjects the beneficiary to the section 204(c) bar which prohibits the approval of a visa petition on his behalf. See 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(ii) (2004). We therefore affirm the decision of the INS acting district director. The petitioner explains that the money paid by the beneficiary to his first wife may have been a loan or a gift and was not explicitly identified as having been in return for engaging in a sham marriage. We agree with the INS acting district director’s thorough discussion of this argument, as well as of the argument that the beneficiary’s first wife may have felt coerced by the INS interview examiner. The petitioner’s statements on appeal do not persuade us that the INS acting district director erred or that his decision should be overturned. For these reasons, we agree with the INS acting district director that the beneficiary’s first marriage was a sham marriage entered into in order to provide an immigration benefit. We further agree with the acting district director that as a result of having entered into a sham marriage, the beneficiary cannot have a visa petition approved on his behalf due to the prohibition contained in section 204(c) of the Act.
 
*3 We next turn to the petitioner’s motion to reopen in which she alleges that she received ineffective assistance from her former attorney regarding the proper filing of her appeal. The petitioner has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Former counsel responded to the petitioner’s allegations by stating that he filed a motion to reconsider with the Board, listing the reasons why he felt that the Board’s decision contained error, and indicating that, following the Board’s February 10, 2004, decision in the beneficiary’s deportation case, he spoke with the beneficiary regarding the various options that were available to him. He also indicated in his response that the Board’s February 10, 2004, decision did not reflect that the Board had considered the motion to reconsider that he had filed. As noted above, the motion to reconsider was not forwarded to the Board for adjudication of the motion. The motion to reconsider was discovered in the record of the visa petition revocation proceedings after the record was forwarded to the Board due to the filing of the petitioner’s May 10, 2004, motion to reopen. We find that the petitioner has not shown that she was prejudiced by her former attorney’s actions because she has not shown that the outcome may have been different but for her former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
Finally, we note that the petitioner includes a variety of documents relating to the merits of her visa petition. She does not claim that these documents are new or previously unavailable or undiscoverable. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Nor does she contend that all of these documents are material to the key issue of whether the beneficiary’s first marriage was entered into in order to evade the immigration laws. Likewise, the petitioner does not argue that these documents would have been submitted to the INS but for the alleged ineffectiveness of former counsel. It therefore appears that the petitioner also seeks to have her visa petition revocation proceedings reopened in order to provide these documents to further support her visa petition. We find that the submission of these documents do not warrant the reopening of the visa petition revocation proceedings because the petitioner does not claim that any alleged ineffectiveness by former counsel prevented these documents from being submitted and has not shown that the documents are new, previously unavailable or undiscoverable, or material to the issue of the bona fides of the beneficiary’s first marriage.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The Board is reconsidering sua sponte our May 15, 2000, decision.
 
*4 FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s May 15, 2000, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The petitioner’s appeal is reinstated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The petitioner’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The petitioner’s June 13, 2000, motion to reconsider is denied as moot.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The petitioner’s May 10, 2004, motion to reopen is denied.

It is standard practice for motions filed in visa petition and visa petition revocation cases to be filed with the INS, now CIS, because that agency is responsible for the collection of fees associated with motions in such cases and is also the agency with custody of the record of proceedings in such cases.

2

It is unnecessary for us to address the merits of this new Notice of Appeal because we have vacated our prior order and reinstated the petitioner’s original appeal.

    0  Review Granted by Luna v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., June 13, 2007
2005 WL 1104617 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTIN RAMIREZ LUNA AMELIA RODRIGUEZ RUIZ
File: AXX XX9 995 - San Francisco
AXX XX5 190
January 26, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Nadeem H. Makada, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Larry J. Gallagher
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On October 26, 2004, this Board dismissed the respondents’ appeal. The respondents now move the Board to reopen the removal proceedings claiming ineffective assistance of their prior counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondents contend that their former counsel did not represent them adequately before the Immigration Judge during the hearing on their applications for cancellation of removal.
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we stated that “before allegations of ineffectiveness are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion” (emphasis added). We noted that the high standard which we enunciated was necessary if the Board is to have a basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that come before us. Id. It is crucial that former counsel be given “the opportunity to respond to the allegation¿sº of ineffectiveness,” due to the charges made by the respondents. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (notification to counsel is “required” for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and complaint letter to California State Bar with notation “cc” to opposing counsel does not satisfy the requirement).
 
However, we have no statement or affidavit from former counsel, giving her version of events, and thus no way to assess the validity of the claim. See also Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 3369 (3rd Cir. 2003) (A requirement of Lozada includes filing a motion and giving the allegedly ineffective counsel an opportunity to respond). The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has indicated that rigid adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required, see Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir.2002) (Lozada “factors are not rigidly applied, especially when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance”). The record in the instant case does not show “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Former counsel appeared at the hearing with the respondents and submitted substantial evidence on their behalf including an affidavit from the male respondent. Former counsel also made a closing argument on behalf of the respondents.
 
*2 We note that the respondents wrote a letter dated November 23, 2004, to their former counsel notifying her that a complaint with the California Bar had been filed against her. There is no evidence, however, that the respondents notified previous counsel that (1) the respondents filed a motion to reopen with the Board based upon her claimed ineffective assistance, and (2) that she was given the opportunity to respond to the respondents’ motion to reopen. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra. The need for notification and response from former counsel (or report that she was notified but failed to respond) is critical to our meaningful assessment of this claim. Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. See also Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013, 1023 (9th Cir. 2004). In accord, Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9 (3rd Cir. 2004).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: MA LOURDES HUIBONHOA A.K.A. MARIA LOURDES HUIBONHOA A.K.A. MA LOURDES LITTON
File: AXX XX1 616 - San Francisco
January 26, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin J. Lawler, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle Guardino
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; cancellation of removal
 
This case was last before the Board on August 3, 2004, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s May 28, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s applications for cancellation of removal and granting the respondent voluntary departure. On October 8, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel and seeking new relief in the form of adjustment of status based upon the pending visa petition which had been recently filed by her 21-year-old son.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposed the motion, arguing that the respondent is statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status because she overstayed the period of voluntary departure. See section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. However, the respondent asserts that she never received the Board’s decision, and that her attorney of record at the time claims that he never received the decision, either. The respondent explains that she only learned about the decision after the expiration of the voluntary departure period, in the process of seeking adjustment of status through her current attorney.
 
Pursuant to the “Stipulation of Settlement and Remand Order” issued by the United States District Court in the Northern District of California on December 20, 2004, we will vacate our prior order and re-issue our decision with today’s date to restart the period of voluntary departure.¿FN1º
 
Turning now to the respondent’s appeal, we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
With regard to the motion to reopen, the respondent has submitted evidence that her son has filed a visa petition on her behalf (Form I-130), and she has submitted a copy of her application for adjustment as well (Form I-485). Although the petition has not yet been approved, we have held that unless clear ineligibility is apparent in the record, the Board shall generally grant motions to reopen in cases involving an application for adjustment of status filed simultaneously with a visa petition where, as here, approval would make the visa immediately available at the time of filing. See Matter of Garcia, 16 I&N Dec. 653 (BIA 1978), modified on other grounds, Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(2)(i)(B). The DHS has only opposed the motion based upon grounds which have been mooted by the district court stipulation. Accordingly, the motion will be granted.
 
*2 ORDER: The Board’s August 3, 2004, decision is hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2005 WL 1104560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JEFFREY BROWN OWEN
File: AXX XX6 402 - Los Angeles
January 25, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Valerie Curtis-Diop, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey C. Finnegan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; adjustment of status
 
The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has appealed an Immigration Judge’s decision granting the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings and granting him adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. The respondent has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The record reflects that the respondent, a 55-year-old native and citizen of New Zeland, entered the United States as a visitor for pleasure, but remained beyond the authorized period of time. On January 28, 1998, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure under section 240B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c, until May 28, 1998. On May 24, 1998, the respondent married his United States citizen wife. Subsequently, on May 27, 1998, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and a request for a stay of removal with the Immigration Court. The Immigration Judge granted the stay of removal on May 28, 1998.
 
The DHS opposed the motion to reopen arguing, inter alia, that the motion was untimely filed and that the respondent was barred from applying for adjustment because he had overstayed his voluntary departure period. Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), aff’d, Shaar v. INS, 141 F.3d 953 (9th Cir. 1998). The record also reflects that the Immigration Judge gave the respondent several opportunities to supplement his motion as it lacked specific supporting documentation. The Immigration Judge eventually granted the respondent’s motion to reopen without a written decision, and granted the respondent’s application for adjustment of status as the spouse of a United States citizen. The DHS’s appeal follows.
 
Upon review of the record, we note that the Immigration Judge erred in granting the respondent’s motion to reopen. As a preliminary matter, the motion to reopen was untimely filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (providing that a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final admnistrative decision was rendered). In addition, the respondent was statutorily ineligible to pursue adjustment of status because he had failed to depart from the United States within his previously-granted period of voluntary departure and presented no evidence that the DHS had extended that voluntary departure period. Matter of Shaar, supra.
 
*2 By statute, an alien who is permitted to depart voluntarily and fails to depart during the time period specified is ineligible to pursue adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, as well as other specified forms of relief. Section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(a); see also Matter of Shaar, supra. Authority to extend the period of voluntary departure after it has been granted by either the Immigration Judge or the Board is only within the jurisdiction of the DHS. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f). A stay request is entirely unrelated to request for an extension of the period of voluntary departure. Compare 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f) (relating to extension of voluntary departure period) with 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f) (relating to requests for a stay pending resolution of a motion).
 
Regardless of whether the respondent’s prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel, the Immigration Judge may not grant reopening to allow the respondent to seek relief for which he is statutorily ineligible. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent’s motion before the Immigration Judge contained no evidence that the respondent’s voluntary departure period had been extended by the DHS, and he therefore became statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status once he failed to depart during the original voluntary departure period. Although we are sympathetic to the respondent’s plight, we are without authority to provide a remedy. Consequently, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied, and the Immigration Judge’s decision granting the application for adjustment of status will be vacated.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied, and the Immigration Judge’s decision granting the application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act is vacated.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Board Member Juan P. Osuna respectfully dissents without opinion.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
