ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is dismissed. The transcript of proceedings shows that the respondent, through counsel, waived appeal (Tr. at 13). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had waived appeal. On September 27, 2004, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26).
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal). The respondent makes no claim that the appeal waiver was invalid.
 
The respondent has filed motions arguing that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at the removal hearing, because his former counsel did not pursue cancellation of removal under 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a) on his behalf, after the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals issued its decision in Cazarez-Gutierrez v. Ashcroft, 382 F.3d 905 (9th Cir. 2004), days before the final removal hearing (that case limited which crimes are classified as “aggravated felonies”, which preclude such relief). Since we lack jurisdiction to address the respondent’s appeal, we also lack jurisdiction to address the motions filed with the Board subsequent to the respondent’s appeal. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); see also Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). We also note that the respondent has failed to comply with any of the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, under Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. The motions are denied for lack of jurisdiction and the record of proceeding is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

2005 WL 649194 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS LEDESMA-LEDESMA
File: AXX XX7 462 - Las Vegas
January 14, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Seth L. Reszko, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles W. Baccus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent appealed from the Immigration Judge’s March 16, 2004, decision finding that his February 4, 2004, motion was untimely, that he did not comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and that Singh v. INS, 295 F.3d 1037 (9th Cir. 2002), was distinguishable from the respondent’s case. We find the respondent’s assertions to the contrary to be without merit.
 
The record reflects that the respondent and his attorney were personally served with notice of the October 16, 2003, hearing. The respondent did not appear; his attorney did. In an October 23, 2003, motion to reopen, the respondent claimed that his former spouse failed to advise him of the date of the hearing. On December 31, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding no exceptional circumstances.
 
The respondent filed a new motion to reopen on January 22, 2004, claiming that his attorney failed to advise him or remind him of the date of his hearing, and alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. On February 3, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied that motion as number barred.
 
On February 4, 2004, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent filed a motion requesting that the prior motion be considered a Motion to Reconsider and again claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. We note, however, that the respondent’s motion had already been considered and denied by the Immigration Judge prior to the receipt of this motion. Moreover, although the respondent attempted to claim that this was a motion to reconsider, it is clearly a motion to reopen, as it presented new and previously unavailable evidence.
 
On March 16, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the February 4, 2004, motion, noting that it was untimely, and even if timely, should be denied.
 
Upon our review of the record, we find that the February 4, 2004, motion to reopen was actually a separate or new motion from the January 22, 2004, motion to reopen. Although the respondent attempted to use the February 4, 2004, motion to amend the January 22, 2004, motion to reopen, it was received too late. Thus, the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that it was barred by regulation.
 
*2 Turning next to the respondent’s claim that he was ineffectively represented by counsel, we note once again that there is no evidence in the record to support that claim for the respondent had personal service of the hearing notice. Singh v. INS, supra, is therefore distinguishable from this case. Moreover, the respondent did not file a bar complaint in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), until after the Immigration Judge had already denied the motion to reopen. The appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 649182 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FASIPE, BABATUNDE
File: AXX XX2 899 - New York
January 13, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Oltarsh, Jennifer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Virna Wright
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The record reflects that the respondent was ordered deported in absentia on October 1, 1993, and filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on April 23, 2004. In a decision dated April 28, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion. The respondent has appealed that decision. We have jurisdiction pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
The respondent argued on appeal that he was misled by his former counsel that it was not necessary for him to appear for his hearing because he was the recipient of a nonimmigrant visa. He contended that he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and he should not be penalized for the negligent advice given to him by his lawyer. We find that the respondent has failed to establish that reopening of these proceedings based on “exceptional circumstances” is appropriate.
 
A request for rescission of an order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. See section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252B(c)(3)(A). Because a request for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of deportation, it is barred from consideration. See also Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998); Matter of A-A, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) (holding that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act on the basis of exceptional circumstances). The decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed.
 
We further will not toll the statute of limitations for seeking reopening pursuant to Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Therein, the court recognized the doctrine of “equitable tolling” of the statute of limitations regarding the filing of a motion to reopen. The court held that an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. Id. In that case, the court concluded that the alien failed to demonstrate such diligence, and concluded that equitable tolling did not apply. Id.
 
*2 Accordingly, under Iavorski v. INS, supra, the Board must first address whether the respondent was denied due process in these proceedings based upon the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.
 
In the instant case, we have reviewed the documents submitted with the instant motion and find that the respondent has not shown that he was the Victim of negligence. We note that the record contains a complaint filed against the respondent’s former counsel with the New York Bar Association. Therein, the respondent acknowledged that he was aware of his deportation hearing, and indicated that counsel put him under the impression that it was not necessary to attend the hearing because of the availability of the H1-B visa. It is not clear as to exactly what counsel did or said to give the respondent that impression. However, we note that the respondent did not allege in the complaint that he was instructed by counsel that he need not appear for the hearing. It therefore appears that the respondent has not presented sufficient evidence establishing a clear and obvious. case of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Even if the respondent’s counsel was negligent, we cannot find that the respondent exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. The respondent indicated in the New York Bar Association complaint that he was stopped by immigration inspectors in 2002 while attempting to enter the United States. He indicated that the inspectors told him that there was a pending deportation order, but apparently admitted the respondent after the respondent explained to them his belief that his immigration status was lawful. Inasmuch as the respondent was clearly on notice in 2002 that he may have been subject to a 1993 deportation order, it was incumbent upon him to then make prompt inquiries into his status. His failure to do so until 2004 is presumptive proof of his failure to exercise due diligence in seeking reopening of proceedings. Equitable tolling of the statute of limitations is therefore inappropriate.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 649190 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ANTONIO MOJICA MATEHUALA MARIA DEL LOS ANGELES GARCIA SILVA
File: AXX XX4 769 - Los Angeles
AXX XX4 770
January 13, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Noemi Ramirez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on October 29, 2004, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s adverse decision, and granted the respondents voluntary departure. The respondents have filed a timely motion asserting that the Board erred in finding that they had not demonstrated exceptional and extremely unusual hardship for purposes of cancellation of removal, and that they did not have effective assistance of counsel during the proceeding below.
 
That aspect of the respondents’ motion which asserts that the Board committed error in denying their application for cancellation of removal is in the nature of a motion to reconsider, while their assertion that their former representatives afforded them ineffective assistance of counsel is a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The sole form of relief sought by the respondents is cancellation of removal. However, pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure, and who has been provided written notice of the consequences of remaining in the United States, is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief. We find that the respondents are barred from applying for cancellation of removal by section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). An alien who remains in the United States after the scheduled date of departure is statutorily ineligible for discretionary relief. Therefore, because the respondents have remained in the United States after the scheduled date of departure, the respondents are now statutorily ineligible for the relief sought.
 
Moreover, for the respondents’ benefit we note that even if their motion were not otherwise barred, they have not complied with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, including informing either of their former representatives of the allegations against them and affording them an opportunity to respond. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000); Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491 (9th Cir. 1986). As such, this aspect of their motion would not have warranted reopening.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.

IN RE: GRACE IDUN

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James T. Reynolds, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, moves the Board to reconsider our decision dated September 8, 2004. On September 8, 2004, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the decision of the Immigration Judge which denied her motion to reopen. In that decision, the Board addressed the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and found that the respondent had not presented a claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988). The Board did not acknowledge in the September decision that the respondent’s counsel had been reprimanded. We find that the reprimand of the respondent’s counsel is sufficient under these facts to establish a case for ineffective assistance under Lozada, supra. For that reason, the motion to reconsider will be granted. However, the respondent’s motion to reopen filed below was untimely in that it was more than 180 days after the in absentia order of the Immigration Judge. There is no evidence that the respondent was prevented by her attorney’s fraud from complying with the deadline and, therefore, equitable tolling does not apply. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). Accordingly, the decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The decision of the Board dated September 8, 2004, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed.

2005 WL 649139 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALVADOR IBARRA
File: AXX XX5 964 - Phoenix
January 10, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicolmedes E. Suriel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda I. Orji,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 6, 2004, in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on November 19, 2003. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge determined that although the respondent complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), the respondent failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear since the in absentia removal order was a consequence of his own doing. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s conduct did not reflect that he acted diligently and in good faith since he waited until about a week before his hearing to seek immigration advice from an assistant at a tax accounting firm, and he elected not to attend his hearing when the “licenciado” failed to meet him on the morning of the hearing.
 
On appeal and in the motion to reopen, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances due to ineffective assistance of counsel. He asserts that he did not receive the hearing notice until the first week in November 2003, and that he contacted Ms. Ingrid Osses at M.A.S. group in Flagstaff, Arizona, because he had heard that this office assisted people with their immigration matters. Furthermore, the respondent believed that Ms. Osses was an attorney, since she had informed him of this fact. Ms. Osses apparently agreed to represent the respondent in Immigration Court on November 19, 2003, but she failed to meet the respondent at her office as she had promised. As Ms. Osses had all of the respondent’s immigration documents, including the hearing notice with the Immigration Court’s address, he contends that he did not attend the hearing because he did not have the Immigration Court’s address and he did not know what to do in her absence. With his motion to reopen, the respondent submitted numerous documents, including his own affidavit; the affidavit of Dena Neese, an attorney at the Law offices of Nicomedes E. Suriel; and the affidavit of Don Rosales, who employed Ms. Osses at the time of the respondent’s contact with her. Mr. Rosales states that he discovered that Ms. Osses apparently was representing herself as an attorney, and she later left his business without informing anyone and took many client files with her.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996). In the case at bar, the respondent had hired Ms. Osses to represent him before the Immigration Judge, believing her to be an attorney. Furthermore, she failed to meet the respondent to accompany him to the Immigration Court on the day of his hearing, and she apparently had retained all of his immigration papers, including his hearing notice.
 
*2 We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The record in the instant case supports the conclusion that based on the totality of circumstances, the respondent has established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. We disagree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent failed to act diligently and in good faith. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen, and he has complied with all of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. Moreover, the respondent’s reliance, upon Ms. Osses’ perceived expertise was not unreasonable under the circumstances. Based upon the respondent’s motion to reopen and the evidence submitted in support thereof, we find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and the proceedings will be reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 649132 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BUTERA, MUTIJIMA CHRISTINE
File: AXX-XX0-841 - Atlanta
January 10, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrick G. Tzeuton, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leslie Smith Turner
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 17, 2003. In removal proceedings, at which the respondent was present and represented by counsel, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure. The respondent filed a motion to reopen, claiming that her counsel did not explain the full meaning of voluntary departure, and that she fears persecution in her native country. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the respondent’s claims were not fully supported by her affidavit, and that the respondent had not set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Along with her appeal, the respondent submits a motion to reopen and reconsider with an additional affidavit regarding her claim of persecution. She states that she did not file a bar complaint against her former counsel because her efforts were aimed at stopping her removal, and not at disciplinary actions against her former attorney.
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra. See Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105, 107-09 (BIA 1984). In the instant case, the respondent has not met any of these requirements. She has not offered a detailed statement of her agreement with her counsel, she has not shown that she informed her counsel of the allegations against her, and her explanation as to why she did not file a complaint against her former counsel is inadequate. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel. We find no basis to reopen or reconsider her additional evidence in support of her claim of persecution.
 
While the respondent’s appeal was pending before us, she submitted a motion for reconsideration of our order summarily affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision. We have not rendered such an order in her case. We find no basis to address the respondent’s latest motion. Accordingly, the respondent’s motions are denied, and the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2005 WL 649132 (BIA)
End of Document

2005 WL 649142 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZHANG WANG CHENG
File: A75 841 750 - New York
January 10, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vlad Kuzmin, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied.1
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). On March 14, 2002, the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s June 8, 1999, denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen the removal proceedings pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii). The respondent submitted the current motion to reopen, through counsel, a year after the Board dismissed his appeal.
 
Underlying the motion is first a claim of ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former appellate attorney. The respondent in his motion claims that former counsel failed to submit a separate brief and as a result of such failure, the respondent’s appeal was summarily dismissed by the Board. The respondent also asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel during his removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge. In this regard, the respondent asserts that his former counsel failed to notify him of his hearing date which subsequently resulted in an in absentia order of removal being issued by the Immigration Judge on November 18, 1998.
 
The Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has held that the filing deadline for motions with the Board may receive the benefit of “equitable tolling” where an alien has demonstrated not only that their constitutional right to due process have been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir. 2000). Here, however, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he exercised diligence in this matter. Although the respondent correctly notes in his affidavit that his former appellate counsel has been suspended from practicing before the Executive Office for Immigration Review, he respondent has offered no explanation as to why his motion was filed late. The respondent’s original motion papers and subsequent filings do not address why his motion was filed long after the expiration of the 90 days allowed for a motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2005 WL 649120 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENRIQUE AISPURO-PENA
File: AXX XX1 697 - San Francisco
January 7, 2005
*1 DISSENTING OPINION: Lauri Steven Filppu, Board Member
I respectfully dissent.
 
The record shows that the respondent was apprehended while attempting to cross the border near San Diego, California. In order to obtain a change of venue to San Francisco, he admitted the charges in the Notice to Appear (NTA) through prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security, (DHS), thus, was not required to offer proof, through testimony of the arresting officer, to establish its allegations.
 
Nevertheless, Exhibit 9 reflects that the respondent admitted his false citizenship claim when again apprehended by DHS after issuance of the in absentia removal order. The respondent understandably wishes to withdraw his admissions. However, absent a meaningful showing by the respondent that the charges are false, I do not believe he has shown the requisite prejudice to be allowed to withdraw the admissions made by prior counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (requiring a showing of substantive prejudice as part of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
I would dismiss the appeal.
 
<Signature>
Lauri Steven Filppu
Board Member

2005 WL 649128 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIX ANTONIO REYES LANDAVERDE
File: AXX XX0 075 - Los Angeles
January 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 Now before us is the respondent’s motion to reopen, filed October 22, 2004. The motion to reopen will be denied. Previously, in a decision dated July 27, 2004, the Board summarily affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision ordering him removed to El Salvador with an alternative grant of voluntary departure. The respondent was notified, in writing, that he was allowed 30 days from the date of that decision in which to depart the United States voluntarily and further, of the penalties attendant to his failure to do so in the absence of any extension.
 
By means of his current motion, the respondent requests reopening for the purpose of renewing an application for relief from removal and adjustment of status, and asserting ineffective assistance of counsel. In this regard, the Board notes that a motion to reopen must, among other things, state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and must be supported by affidavits or other evidence. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). Such a motion may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In addition, an alien must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought before a motion to reopen will be granted. Matter of Gutierrez, supra.
 
Importantly, in this case the motion was filed well after the last day of the respondent’s voluntary departure period and failed to reflect any extension of voluntary departure time. Pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure, and who has been provided written notice of the consequences of remaining in the United States, is statutorily barred from applying for a period of 10 years for any further relief under this section and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249. There is no statutory exception to this 10-year preclusion of relief under current law. Further, although the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, he does not specifically allege that he was advised by counsel to remain in the United States after the expiration of his voluntary departure period or any extensions thereof. The respondent, thus, is barred from receiving special rule cancellation of removal and adjustment of status because of his failure to comply with the terms of his grant of voluntary departure.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 649119 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ENRIQUE AISPURO-PENA
File: AXX XX1 697 - San Francisco
January 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Samuel U. Ogbu, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii)º - False claim of United States citizenship
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2003, decision ordering him removed from the United States based on concessions and admissions made by a former attorney in a written change of venue motion submitted in September 2001. We note that the respondent’s former attorney initially contested the charges in the Notice to Appear, including the charge under section 212(a)(6)(A)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, i.e., making a false claim of United States citizenship (Tr. at 3). This attorney, Juan Castro, later inexplicably admitted and conceded the allegations and charges in the above-referenced written pleading. Thereafter, the attorney neglected to inform the respondent of a scheduled court date, which resulted first in the entry of an in absentia order of removal, and then in the withdrawal of that order pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s granting of the respondent’s motion to reopen due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See February 5, 2002, and July 22, 2003, orders. As part of the respondent’s application for reopening in which he invoked our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), the respondent’s new attorney submitted materials indicating that the former attorney, Mr. Castro, has resigned from the practice of law. See Exh. 8. Notwithstanding all of the above, the Immigration Judge refused to allow the respondent to enter new pleadings in response to the charges in the Notice to Appeal. In fact, the concession to the highly-prejudicial charge of making a false claim of United States citizenship still stands, which could eliminate the chance of the respondent ever adjusting his status to live with his citizen wife and children. The respondent argued, through his new attorney, that he should not be held to the written pleadings of his former attorney, when those pleadings were inconsistent with the original position taken by the respondent and clearly prejudicial to the respondent’s case. We agree. In this case, given the clear evidence of ineffective assistance provided by Mr. Castro, we do not believe it is appropriate to hold the respondent to pleadings where there is no indication that those pleadings were the result of a carefully considered choice by the respondent, as opposed to careless work by a former attorney whose representation has been called into question. Accordingly, we find it appropriate to vacate the Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2003, order, and remand this case for further proceedings.

2005 WL 649126 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERMINIO GARCIA-HUERTA A.K.A. HERMINIO GARCIA
File: AXX-XX4-420 - Los Angeles
January 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric Pan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sumeet Lall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (withdrawn)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 25, 2004, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on November 6, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has filed an opposition. The appeal will be sustained.
 
We adopt the findings of fact set forth by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 1-2). The Immigration Judge found that a Notice of Hearing was personally served on the respondent’s counsel and that the respondent was orally warned of the consequences of failing to appear for his November 6, 2003, hearing. The Immigration Judge did not specifically find that the respondent was given oral notice of the time of his hearing on November 6, 2003. The respondent asserts that he thought the hearing was at 1:00 p.m. as his prior hearings had been, and claims that his attorney did not give him a copy of the Notice of Hearing or inform him that the hearing time was 8:30 a.m. He submitted an affidavit from his former counsel stating that she did not inform the respondent that his hearing time was 8:30 a.m.
 
An order of removal entered in absentia may be rescinded if the respondent demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Receiving ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute exceptional circumstances. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). The respondent has sufficiently complied with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Specifically, the affidavit of the respondent’s former counsel concedes her obligation to inform the respondent of his hearing time. The respondent has also demonstrated that he filed a bar complaint with respect to this matter. The respondent attended all of his prior hearings, including a merits hearing at which he presented the testimony of witnesses, he has demonstrated that he lacked actual notice of the time of the hearing due to his counsel’s failure to inform him of such, he is a long-time resident of the United States, and he appears prima facie eligible for relief from removal. Under these circumstances, we conclude that counsel’s failure to ensure that the respondent received a copy of the Notice of Hearing or to otherwise inform him that his hearing time was 8:30 a.m. constitutes exceptional circumstances warranting rescission of the in absentia removal order and reopening of his removal proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2005 WL 649088 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN MADRIGAL-ALVARADO
File: A70 166 129 - Los Angeles
January 5, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Philippe Dwelshauvers, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen and will be denied. This is the third motion to reopen filed in the instant case. Two prior motions were denied by the Immigration Judge on March 9, 1999, and on February 14, 2001, respectively.1 The regulations provide that motions to reopen shall be limited to one motion to reopen, with certain exceptions not pertinent here. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion to reopen is therefore barred by numerical limitations.
 
The respondent makes a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and claims he is eligible to have his case equitably tolled due to the ineffective assistance he received from a notary public holding himself out as an attorney. The respondent has filed an affidavit attesting to the chain of events leading up to the instant motion. We note also, that because the respondent’s former representative was not an attorney, there is no need for further compliance with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We note also, for an untimely motion to reopen to receive the benefit of equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must exercise due diligence in pursuing the case during the period to be tolled. The last order issued in this case was in 2002. Inasmuch as the respondent has waited 2 years before filing the instant motion, he has not exercised due diligence in pursuing remedies available to him with regards to the alleged ineffective assistance violations.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The final administrative order was issued by the Immigration Judge on December 16, 1998, when he granted the respondent 40 days within which to voluntarily leave the United States. While the Board did issue an order on November 21, 2002, denying respondent’s appeal, the appeal that was pending before the Board was not an appeal of an underlying merits decision, but rather, an appeal of a denial of the earlier motion to reopen. Because respondent’s motion to reopen was never granted, he is still under the final administrative order of deportation issued by the Immigration Judge.

2004 WL 3187263 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUCIO CORTEZ ARJONA
File: AXX XX0 950 - San Francisco
December 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hilari Allred, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen or reconsider
 
*1 In an order entered on May 26, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings on October 7, 2004. The Department of Homeland Security has not registered opposition to the motion, and it will be granted.
 
The respondent argues that his former counsel, David A. Diaz, provided ineffective assistance of counsel. It is argued that Mr. Diaz failed to obtain and present pertinent evidence regarding the hardship to the respondent’s qualifying relatives, regarding his application for cancellation of removal. It is also maintained that Mr. Diaz failed to file an appellate brief, although he had been retained to do so. Further, it is alleged that Mr. Diaz failed to inform the respondent of the Board’s order, which resulted in the expiration of the period of voluntary departure.
 
The respondent has provided a printout of punitive action taken against Mr. Diaz by the California State Bar, of which he is a member. The printout reveals several actions in the last several years, in which Mr. Diaz has been ordered inactive or suspended outright from the practice of law. On June 12, 2004, Mr. Diaz was ordered inactive and then “not entitled.” On September 16, 2004, Mr. Diaz was suspended.
 
Generally, respondents in immigration proceedings are bound by the actions of their counsel, absent egregious circumstances. In this case, Mr. Diaz’s record as a practicing attorney in California gives rise to a question as to the quality of representation that he provided the respondent, particularly in view of the dire consequences of failing voluntarily to depart.
 
The respondent has raised serious allegations concerning the quality of Mr. Diaz’s representation, and he has substantially complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, we deem it appropriate to grant the motion to reopen and remand this case for another hearing. The respondent should be afforded the opportunity to present evidence regarding his eligibility and worthiness for the discretionary relief of cancellation of removal. We note that the respondent has presented compelling evidence with his motion regarding hardship to a qualifying relative for purposes of cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge should issue a new decision either granting or denying the requested relief. The respondent’s removability is settled and need not be revisited.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the appeal is reinstated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s order of May 26, 2004, is vacated; except as to the issue of removability, the Immigration Judge’s decision is also vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the issuance of a new decision.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YARI, ABOUBAKAR
File: AXX-XX2-449 - Atlanta

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent submitted a motion to reopen his proceedings conducted in absentia, claiming that he switched the dates for his state court hearing and his immigration court hearing in his notebook, and that he was absent due to this error. On October 24, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding that the respondent had not established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. Seven months later, the respondent filed a motion for reconsideration and a motion for sua sponte reopening. He claimed that his right to due process was violated because he was forced to run between his state court proceedings and his immigration court proceedings. He claimed that he was not placed in immigration proceedings for a violation of the immigration laws, but rather on the basis of the withdrawal of the visa petition filed on his behalf and his pursuit of a child protection order in state court. He claims that errors by his former attorney denied him the opportunity to pursue relief from removal, and that his failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. On August 3, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the motions. He found that the respondent had proper notice of his hearing, that his scheduling error did not establish exceptional circumstances, and that the motion for reconsideration was not timely filed. We agree. We further find that the respondent has not shown that he is prima facie eligible for relief from removal, and he has not presented a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has failed to establish a basis for reconsideration. He has not shown that sua sponte reopening is warranted in his case. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 3187225 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANKIE CONTRERAS A.K.A. JOSE FRANCISCO CONTRERAS1
File: A94 148 422 - Los Angeles
December 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ana Avila Cote, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason R. Grimm
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Relief under NACARA; remand
 
The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s decision, dated March 12, 2001, denying his application for relief under the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act (“NACARA”), Title II of Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193 (1997), amended by Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644 (1997). In addition, the respondent has filed a motion to remand claiming that he is eligible for adjustment of status and also claiming that his request to reinstate his appeal before the Board was due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied. The appeal is dismissed.
 
Initially, the Board will address the motion to remand. The respondent has two arguments contending that a remand is appropriate. First, he claims that he is now eligible for adjustment of status and should be given the opportunity to pursue that form of relief. He has indicated that the visa petition filed by his mother-in-law, Mrs. Angela Estrada, included him as well as his wife and that he is the beneficiary of an approved “family petition.” The evidence of record shows that his wife, Zoe M. Contreras, was the only beneficiary listed on Ms. Estrada’s Form I-797. We note that the respondent could not qualify as a beneficiary in any visa petition filed by his mother-in-law because family-sponsored immigrants include only spouses, sons, daughters, brothers, and sisters of a citizen. See section 203(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The petition for Zoe was approved and the respondent’s wife became a legal permanent resident of the United States in September 2004. However, there is no evidence of an approved visa petition, let alone the filing of such a petition, by the respondent’s wife with him listed as the beneficiary. See Hernandez v. Ashcroft, 345 F.3d 824 (9th Cir. 2003) (as the spouse of a lawful permanent resident, an alien is eligible for an immigrant visa under the second family preference category upon approval of the petition); 8 U.S.C. § 1153(a)(2)(A). The respondent has not forwarded an application for adjustment of status relative to himself in support of the motion to remand. Accordingly, the motion to remand to pursue adjustment of status is denied.
 
*2 Second, the respondent argues that his motion to remand should be granted because the reinstatement of his appeal before the Board amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel and that he has fulfilled all of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Board finds that the respondent has not satisfied all the Lozada requirements in that he failed to notify his former attorney of the complaint filed against him and to give that individual an opportunity to respond to the allegations against him. Although the evidence shows that the respondent has filed a complaint against his former attorney with the State Bar of California, there is no evidence that he notified the attorney directly of the allegations. Even if the State Bar of California, in its investigation of the complaint, notifies the attorney of the allegations leveled against him, Lozada requires that the alien notify the attorney himself and give the attorney an opportunity to respond to the allegations against him.
 
Even if we were to find that the respondent substantially complied with the requirements in Lozada, the evidence does not support a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent’s prior attorney was making a strategic decision in advising him to make a motion to reinstate his appeal before the Board. Moreover, the respondent has admitted that he previously applied for temporary protected status (TPS) in April 1991 and his application was denied because of abandonment. See respondent’s Motion to Remand, dated November 3, 2004. Accordingly, the Board’s June 26, 2001, order administratively closing the proceedings before it to allow the respondent the opportunity to apply for TPS was actually affording the respondent a second chance to apply for TPS, when the record now shows that he had already applied for such relief and it had been denied. Accordingly, the motion to remand to pursue a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is denied as well.
 
Pursuant to the Board’s decision dated June 3, 2003, the respondent’s appeal of the March 12, 2001, Immigration Judge’s decision has been reinstated and is currently before us. The Immigration Judge denied relief under NACARA, finding that the respondent lacked good moral character based on substantial criminal activity. We agree that, based on the respondent’s prior criminal convictions, he lacks the requisite good moral character to be eligible for relief under NACARA. In 1992, the respondent was involved in an incident that resulted in a conviction for assault with a deadly weapon, gross bodily injury likely. He was found guilty, convicted, and sentenced to 90 days in jail and 24 months probation. Although this conviction ultimately was set aside and dismissed, the fact remains that he was found guilty and convicted of assault with a deadly weapon. The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that expunged convictions can be used in assessing an alien’s good moral character because the facts underlying expunged convictions are relevant in the context of good moral character determinations. See United States. v. Hovsepian, 359 F.3d 1144, 1158-58 (9th Cir. 2004); see also Ramirez-Castro v. INS, 287 F.3d 1172 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
*3 We find that the Immigration Judge did not err in determining that the respondent did not have the good moral character required for relief under NACARA. Therefore, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for such relief and the application for NACARA was correctly denied.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent has been in immigration proceedings before under the name Jose Contreras-Salinas, A29 179 986. He has admitted that he was deported on April 14, 1989, and reentered the United States 2 weeks later. See respondent’s Motion to Remand, dated November 3, 2004.

2004 WL 3187234 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOHNSON CHIBIKO OBIA
File: A78 107 181 - Dallas
December 27, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear at the hearing on February 7, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent claims that he never received notice of the hearing. He also asserts that he established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear.
 
The record reflects that the notice of hearing was mailed to the respondent’s last known address. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he did not receive the notice of hearing that was mailed to his address. Furthermore, there is no evidence in the record of proceedings that the respondent was represented by attorney Augustine Uke, since a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) was never submitted to the Immigration Court.
 
In his motion to reopen filed on May 12, 2004, the respondent claimed ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. However, the respondent failed to file his motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the final order, as is required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act).1 Moreover, the respondent failed to comply with all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). With his motion to reopen, the respondent submitted a State Bar of Texas Grievance Form. However, the respondent failed to submit an affidavit as required, nor did he submit evidence that his former counsel was informed of the allegations of ineffective assistance and given an opportunity to respond. Furthermore, there is no EOIR-28 form in the record of proceedings to indicate that Mr. Uke was representing the respondent before the Immigration Court. This is not a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel that may excuse strict compliance with the Lozada requirements. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear.
 
*2 On appeal the respondent also asserts that he is eligible for adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).2 Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).3 Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision. He is accordingly precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.4 Furthermore, the record reflects that the respondent received oral warnings of the consequences of failing to appear at a scheduled hearing. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings pursuant to section 242B of the Act. However, as section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act sets forth identical language to that contained in section 242B(c)(3), we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, by analogy.

2

We note that Matter of M-S-, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings and involved the interpretation of sections 242B(e)(1) and (5) of the Act, relating to the limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a deportation hearing. However, as section 240(b)(7) of the Act sets forth a similar limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a removal hearing, we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of M-S-, supra, by analogy.

3

Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3), a motion to reopen for the purpose of acting on an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.

4

We therefore decline to address the issue of prima facie eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the respondent would deserve a favorable exercise of discretion.

2004 WL 3187214 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ALFREDO RIVERA
File: A79 522 032 - Los Angeles
December 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan R. Diamante, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Motion to remand; cancellation of removal
 
The respondent, a male native and citizen of Mexico, appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 4, 2003. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged, and denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel who represented him during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.1
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal because the respondent failed to complete his background and fingerprint checks in a timely fashion (I.J. at 4-5, 7). The Immigration Judge also noted lack of diligence on the part of the respondent’s counsel in filing his motion to withdraw as counsel with the Immigration Court (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge denied the motion to withdraw for having been filed too close to the hearing date, and for the attorney’s failure to adequately explain the reasons for his request to withdraw (I.J. at 4, 6-7).
 
The respondent contends that he fell victim to ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he alleges that his previous counsel failed to properly prepare for the hearing, and failed to timely file with the Immigration Court evidence which was requested by the Immigration Judge. He also contends that he cooperated fully and diligently with his attorney in gathering and submitting the necessary evidence. He indicated that he paid his former attorney a sum of money to complete the necessary work, and he relied on his attorney to complete and file with the Court the necessary documents. The respondent submitted a copy of an April 2, 2003, payment receipt from his prior attorney, indicating that this money was paid to prepare for the upcoming hearing. He also submitted a copy an April 2, 2003, receipt from the Sheriff’s Office, reflecting payment for a fingerprint card. The respondent indicated that he took the fingerprint card directly to his former attorney’s office. Moreover, the respondent submitted evidence reflecting that the California State Bar Court recently recommended that the attorney against whom the respondent claims ineffective assistance be disbarred for having rendered ineffective assistance to numerous of his immigration clients. The respondent’s former attorney was also recently suspended from practice before this Board.
 
*2 Overall, the record of proceedings reflects that the respondent’s application for cancellation removal was denied for failure to timely submit to the Immigration Court background and fingerprint checks. The evidence also indicates that such failure may have been the result of ineffective assistance of the respondent’s prior counsel. As such, the record reveals that the respondent suffered prejudice. In view of the foregoing, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

1

2004 WL 3187220 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BURAN CANKA
File: A75 835 770 - New York
December 22, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mariusz Dragan, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On September 11, 2003, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied his motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia.1 The respondent filed a late motion to reopen on August 2, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “‘DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be reminded only upon a motion to reopen, filed with the Immigration Judge within 180 days, which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The Board is precluded from considering such a challenge to an in absentia order in the first instance. See Matter of Guzman, supra. The Board has jurisdiction to consider an appeal, when a motion to reopen an in absentia order is denied by an Immigration Judge. Id. As stated, we considered, and denied, the respondent’s appeal from such a motion in this case. The respondent now seeks to have the Board reconsider its decision, based on a claim that 2 prior attorneys were ineffective.
 
The Immigration Judge, as affirmed by this Board, determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen filed with the judge was untimely, as it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia decision. The Immigration Judge also concluded that the respondent had failed to show exceptional circumstances excusing his failure to appear at the removal hearing. The respondent argues in the pending motion that prior counsel erred in not filing the motion timely with the Immigration Judge, and that a second attorney provided ineffective assistance by not promptly bringing a complaint of ineffective assistance of counsel against the first attorney.
 
Even were we to agree that prior counsel erred by not filing a timely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, and even were to consider this claim despite the lateness of the pending motion, we would find that the respondent has still failed to show exceptional circumstances for missing the February 27, 2001, removal hearing. The respondent asserts that he was not able to arrive on time for the hearing, because he was involved in an automobile accident. The lead respondent’s affidavit does not provide sufficient evidence to conclude that the respondent was unable to appear for the removal hearing, which was scheduled for 9 a.m. on February 27, 2001. See e.g. Matter of S-A-, 21 I&N Dec. 1050 (general assertion that heavy traffic precluded attendance at hearing does not provide reasonable cause for reopening). The respondent indicates that the accident caused damage to the vehicle, and rendered his automobile inoperable, but there is no suggestion that the respondent or his wife was injured. A receipt from an automobile collision company, which towed the car, states that the car was picked up at 8:30 a.m. The respondent fails to explain why, despite the fact that he was apparently uninjured, he could not thereafter either arrive at the immigration court by 9:00 a.m. or alert his counsel or the immigration court that he and his wife would be late. The respondent has not provided sufficient evidence to reopen the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to establish that exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance at the February 27, 2001, removal hearing. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2004 WL 3187194 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NAIDU, ABINESH NARAYAN
File: AXX-XX1-187 - Lancaster
December 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lal, Hari S., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paul R. Foster,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 25, 2004. First, we reject the respondent’s claim that he is entitled to a remand because of the ineffective assistance of prior counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring alien to set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel; that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and that the alien indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not). Although the respondent appears to meet the above-mentioned procedural requirements for asserting a so-called Lozada claim based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not shown how prior counsel’s failure to request a trial date or to adequately prepare his case caused any prejudice to him. This is true because the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (i.e., an alien convicted of a crime of violence). The respondent’s March 2002, conviction for committing lewd and lascivious acts upon a child under the age of 14 constitutes both sexual abuse of a minor (for which the respondent was not charged) and a crime of violence (which is charged in the Notice to Appear). See Exh. 2 (record of the respondent’s conviction under § 288(a) of the California Penal Code); see also U.S. v. Baron-Medina, 187 F.3d 1144 (9th Cir. 1999) (conviction under §288(a) of the California Penal Code is sexual abuse of a minor); U.S. v. Pereira-Salmeron, 337 F.3d 1148 (9th Cir. 2003) (conviction for sexual abuse of a minor is a crime of violence for purposes of U.S. Sentencing Guidelines). We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit’s decision in U.S. v. Pereira-Salmeron, supra, has application beyond the sentencing guidelines, given the court’s overarching observation of the risk of force that is “inherent” when “an older person attempts to sexually touch a child under the age of fourteen.” U.S. v. Pereira-Salmeron, supra, at 1154 (citations omitted). Cf. Matter of Malta-Espinoza, 23 I&N Dec. 656 (BIA 2004). Because the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony as charged, and is consequently ineligible for the relief of cancellation of removal, voluntary departure, and a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, he has not shown any prejudice that may have resulted from the alleged ineffective assistance of prior counsel. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 3187212 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHAMAN SINGH
File: AXX XX7 465 - New York City
December 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaspreet Singh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mary Cheng,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated April 20, 204, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. The respondent has appealed the decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was ordered deported in absentia, based on his failure to appear for a scheduled hearing on March 19, 2004. On April 15, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had notice of the hearing and that he failed to establish that exceptional circumstances caused his failure to appear. Subsequently, the respondent filed this appeal. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen because he did demonstrate exceptional circumstances prevented him from appearing on time.
 
The respondent indicated in his motion to reopen that he appeared on time for his hearing before the Immigration Judge. He stated that his former attorney was not present so he waited outside the courtroom for his attorney to arrive. The respondent waited for his attorney for an hour past his scheduled appearance time, and was deported in absentia. He argued that he should not be penalized on account of his counsel’s dereliction of duty.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because she did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because she was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
The Second Circuit has not addressed this issue directly. In Romero-Morales v. INS, 25 F.3d 125 (2d Cir. 1994), the court remanded for the Immigration Judge to consider the circumstances evidenced in the motion to change venue in connection with the motion to reopen an in absentia hearing. In another case, the First Circuit has found that the totality of the circumstances must be considered in deciding whether exceptional circumstances warrant the reopening of a decision in absentia to deport an alien who failed to appear. Herbert v. Ashcroft, 325 F.3d 68 (1st Cir. 2003). In that case the alien was 30 minutes late for his hearing at which his counsel did not appear, because counsel had another hearing before a federal magistrate. The Ninth Circuit has found that in the case of an alien who is merely late, it cannot be said that the alien “failed to appear,” and it is an abuse of discretion to refuse to reopen or to continue proceedings. Jerezano v. INS, 169 F.3d 613 (9th Cir. 1999). The Seventh Circuit has held that an alien showed exceptional circumstances where an alien waited for her interpreter, who was late. Nazarova v. INS, 171 F.3d 478, (7th Cir. 1999).
 
*2 We have considered the totality of circumstances surrounding the respondent’s case. We note that the respondent has not indicated that he was instructed by his attorney or anyone else to not enter the courtroom until the arrival of his counsel. He has also not filed a filed a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in accordance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In addition, the respondent has no record of previous appearances in the Immigration Court. We find that, considering this record as a whole, he has not shown exceptional circumstances prevented him from appearing on time, and the respondent’s motion to reopen should be denied.
 
Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed, and the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 3187312 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NUNES, RICARDO EMANUEL
File: A38 580 764 - Newark
December 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
AMENDED ORDER1
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reconsider our decision dated May 13, 2004. Applicable regulations provide that motions to reopen removal proceedings “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.…” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is eligible as a matter of law for the relief sought upon reopening. Matter of Rodriguez-Vera, 17 & N Dec.105 (BIA 1979). A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
The respondent’s instant motion is based on (1) our failure to grant his timely-filed motion for an extension in the briefing schedule on his appeal, and (2) a claim that ineffective assistance of counsel in the underlying criminal proceedings led to the imposition of a one-year sentence on a probation violation, and, hence, the respondent’s designation as an aggravated felon under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act. At bottom, we assume that the respondent claims that absent the 1-year sentence, he would not have been designated an aggravated felon, that the removal charge would not have been sustained, and that proceedings would have been terminated. As to the first basis for the motion to reopen, the respondent has not made a prima facie showing that he would likely have been granted relief if we had considered his brief on appeal. The respondent has not submitted a copy of the brief or indicated what additional information the appeal brief would have contained beyond the 6-page “Affirmation in Support of the Notice of Appeal” submitted with the appeal on February 11, 2004, which basically raised the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Notably, the respondent has not indicated that the brief would have included the necessary elements to make a cognizable claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under rules previously articulated by this Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (B1A), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring that the motion be supported by an affidavit by the alien setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel, including relevant representations; that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not). The respondent failed to meet these threshold requirements both in the underlying appeal and in his instant motion. At a minimum, we note that even at this point in these proceedings, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit swearing that his counsel in the criminal proceedings failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his plea to one of the numerous alleged probation violations. Therefore, even if we were to reopen proceedings, the ineffective assistance of counsel claim would be deficient. In this regard, we reject the respondent’s reliance, in the motion to reopen, on his pending motion in New York state court to set aside his one-year criminal sentence. A pending motion to reduce an underlying criminal sentence falls far short of showing clear evidence of prejudicial ineffective assistance of counsel during the removal proceedings below. See Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3d Cir. 2003).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
