U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDMOND MADANI
File: AXX XX8 246 - New York
December 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James D. Christo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura A. Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before this Board on September 7, 2004, when we denied a motion to reconsider filed by the respondent. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen, based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, on October 4, 2004. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Board issued its administratively final decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal on June 4, 2004. The motion is untimely. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent claims that prior counsel was incompetent, because prior counsel failed to file a brief with this Board, which caused us to summarily dismiss his appeal. Even were the motions deadline tolled, the motion would be denied. As the DHS argues, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 110 (2d Cir. 1993). Here, there is no evidence that the respondent gave the attorney the opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, supra. Consequently, there is no statement or affidavit from counsel, describing why she did not file a brief in support of the appeal. The motion asserts that “¿hºis former counsel has refused to respond to his requests for an explanation for her failure to timely file the brief …” but no proof is submitted that former counsel was notified of the charges against her. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

IN RE: FELIX CRUZ-FIGUEREDO

December 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eugenia Ruiz-Uribe,
Accredited Representative
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on May 3, 2004, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision for failure to file an appellate brief where the respondent had indicated that he would do so in his Notice of Appeal. The respondent has timely filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel and seeking relief in the form of adjustment of status under section 1 of the Cuban Refugee Adjustment Act of November 2, 1966, Pub. L. No. 89-732, 80 Stat. 1161, as amended (“Cuban Adjustment Act”). As the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the respondent’s motion, it is deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion, and for the entry, of a new decision.
 
We find that the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We further find that the respondent has demonstrated that he received ineffective assistance of counsel as his former attorney indicated that he would file an appeal brief in this matter but failed to file a brief with this Board, and the failure to file such brief prejudiced the respondent’s case.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Cuba, was last paroled into the United States. See Form I-213 Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien dated December 4, 2000. The respondent attempted to apply for adjustment of status before the Immigration Court, but he was informed by the Immigration Judge that submission of an adjusment application was unnecessary as the appropriate mechanism for adjustment under the Cuban Adjustment Act was termination, which required agreement from the DHS (Tr. at 25-26). See also Cuban Adjustment Act. However, termination does not require agreement from the DHS, whereas administrative closure does. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 203 (BIA 1990). Moreover, this Board has held that an Immigration Judge has jurisdiction to adjudicate an application for adjustment of status under the Cuban Adjustment Act where an otherwise eligible arriving alien in removal proceedings is paroled into this country. See Matter of Artigas, 23 I&N Dec. 99 (BIA 2001). The respondent could have argued the holding in Matter of Artigas, supra, in an appeal brief, and we note that the respondent has submitted such a brief with his motion to reopen.
 
*2 The respondent has established prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under the Cuban Adjustment Act as he was paroled into this country after January 1, 1959; he has been physically present in the United States for at least 1 year; and, he appears admissible and eligible for an immigrant visa.1 We conclude that these proceedings must be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of prior counsel, and the respondent will be allowed to apply for any form of relief for which he is eligible. As there do not appear from the current record to be negative factors that would militate against a favorable exercise of discretion, we will reopen this matter and the record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.

IN RE: PAULA GABRIELA IBARRA NAVA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on February 5, 2003. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on June 3, 2004. The respondent was granted 30 days to voluntarily depart the United States. On August 17, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, claiming to have new evidence concerning her request for cancellation of removal under section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). We denied the motion to reopen on October 14, 2004. We found that the respondent was ineligible for cancellation of removal, as she remained in the United States beyond the voluntary departure period. See section 240B(d) of the Act. The respondent has filed a motion which, in essence, seeks to have the Board reconsider its October 14, 2004, decision, and claims that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is denied, as we correctly determined that she became statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal because she failed to voluntarily depart the United States, as ordered. See section 240B(d) of the Act. Although the respondent claims that former counsel provided ineffective assistance by not submitting a timely motion to reopen, in fact the motion to reopen was timely filed. The respondent does not demonstrate that former counsel is to blame for her failure to depart, as ordered. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2004 WL 3187321 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RANI KAUR HAYER
File: AXX XX6 765 - San Francisco
December 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander H. Lubarsky, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Katrinka J. Blunt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent asserts on appeal that her half hour delay in appearing before the Immigration Judge did not constitute a failure to appear. She also asserts that the Immigration Judge was without jurisdiction to order her removed to India because she had departed the United States between her first and second master hearing.
 
The Ninth Circuit, in which this case arises, has held that an Immigration Judge has discretion to determine that an alien’s late arrival for a hearing is excusable, and should not be characterized as a failure to appear. See Jerezano v. INS, 169 F.3d 613 (9th Cir. 1999). The Immigration Judge may exercise that discretion either on the day of the hearing, or while adjudicating a motion to reopen. If an Immigration Judge determines in the exercise of discretion that an alien’s lateness is excusable, the requirement to show “exceptional circumstances” for reopening is not required.
 
In this case, the Immigration Judge found that the alien’s late arrival was not excusable and did constitute a failure to appear. The Immigration Judge further concluded that there was no exceptional circumstance for that failure to appear. We do not find that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion in the exercise of discretion. The respondent’s decision to assume that the time listed on her notice of hearing was incorrect, rather than obtain clarification from the court, did not amount to exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. Moreover, this was the second time the respondent had failed to appear for a removal proceeding.
 
We further find, to the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel, that the respondent was not prejudiced by her attorney’s actions, as there appears to be little likelihood that reopening would change the result in this case. The respondent’s claim that the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction is without merit. Removal proceedings were commenced by the filing of the Notice to Appear with the Immigration Court on July 2, 2003. See 8 C.F.R. § 1239.1(a) (2004). This was also the point at which jurisdiction vested with the Immigration court. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.14(a) (2004). As the respondent did not depart the United States until August 20, 2003, the Immigration Judge had jurisdiction at the time of the respondent’s departure, and the September 25, 2003, in absentia order was proper. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 3187321 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2004 WL 3187300 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TIAN LI
File: A42 956 424 - San Francisco
December 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Allan A. Samson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jayme Salinardi,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 14, 2003. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.
 
On October 18, 1995, the respondent was convicted of possessing an access card with intent to defraud. He was sentenced to three years in prison. The Immigration Judge found this to constitute an aggravated felony. See 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G)(1997). Consequently, he is ineligible for cancellation of removal. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(a)(3), 1229b(b)(1)(C)(1997). Because the respondent was found guilty following a jury trial, he is ineligible for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See INS v. St. Cyr, 121 S.Ct. 2271 (2001); Armendariz-Montoya v. Sonchik, 291 F.3d 1116 (9th Cir. 2002); 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c)(1995).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that his former attorney committed ineffective assistance of counsel by withdrawing the respondent’s request for withholding of removal. We first note that the respondent has failed to show that he is even prima facie eligible for withholding of removal. The respondent failed to allege facts that, if true, would indicate a clear probability that his life or freedom would be threatened on account of a protected ground if he is returned to China. See INS v. Stevic, 467 U.S. 407 (1984); 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3) (1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16 (2003).
 
In addition, we note that the respondent has failed to submit evidence that he has fulfilled the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has failed to attach to his brief a sworn affidavit, signed by the respondent, setting out the alleged facts necessary to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. This affidavit should have included the allegations presented in respondent’s brief that the respondent’s prior counsel had erred in withdrawing the respondent’s request for withholding of removal.1 The sworn affidavit should have also included the contractual agreement with his former counsel concerning his withholding claim. The sworn affidavit should have also indicated that he has notified his former counsel of the allegation of ineffective assistance, enabling the former counsel to respond to the allegations. The sworn affidavit should have also indicated that a complaint has been filed against his former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Grijalva, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra. He also failed to show prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, supra. Because these prerequisites were not fulfilled, the respondent has failed to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

Because the factual statements in the motion are not sworn to be true, they are not evidence. Motions or briefs, containing legal argument, must be accompanied by a sworn statement of a person having personal knowledge of the facts alleged. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Cf. Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1993); Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 1983).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Mendoza-Mazariegos v. Mukasey, 9th Cir., December 6, 2007
2004 WL 3187304 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE MARIO MENDOZA-MAZARIEGOS
File: A91 973 031 - Los Angeles
December 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nana Y. Boachie-Yiadom, Esquire
*1 In a decision dated September 18, 2003, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ordered his removal, should he fail to avail himself of voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge also determined that the respondent had failed to show eligibility for cancellation of removal. The respondent appealed the decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent has failed to demonstrate that he qualifies for cancellation of removal, because he has failed to prove he has been a person of good moral character during the 10 years immediately preceding the date of his application, and he has failed show that he has not been convicted of certain offenses which would render him ineligible for that relief. See section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1129b(b)(1).
 
The respondent contends, on the appeal form, that he was allowed insufficient time to prepare and present his case, and that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to grant a continuance to permit his newly-retained counsel to prepare to represent him.1 A review of the record confirms, however, that the respondent had ample time to prepare his case and, on the date of the merits hearing, he had been granted a two-year continuance for the sole purpose of preparation of the cancellation application.
 
Proceedings in this case commenced in July 1998. At a hearing on August 7, 1998, the respondent stated that he wished to apply for cancellation, and an application form was provided. At a hearing on August 17, 1998, an Immigration Judge reported that the application had been submitted, and a merits hearing was scheduled. However, when the hearing convened on September 8, 1998, it was discovered that the respondent had been issued the wrong application form; he was provided with the correct form, and the hearing was rescheduled. Venue was changed to Los Angeles, and at a subsequent hearing, the respondent had retained local counsel, who explained that the cancellation application was not ready to file. The Immigration Judge granted a continuance, and the application was submitted at a hearing on February 11, 1999; at that time, a merits hearing was scheduled.
 
On July 3, 2000, counsel was not available, and the Immigration Judge continued proceedings. On October 10, 2001, the respondent’s hearing was delayed over a scheduling matter, and the Immigration Judge continued the case until September 18, 2003; when the hearing was convened on that date, the respondent reported that he had retained new counsel “yesterday”. The Immigration Judge granted the newly-retained counsel’s request to withdraw and proceeded with the pro se hearing.
 
*2 In view of the numerous and lengthy continuances which were granted in this case, we conclude that the Immigration Judge was well within his discretion to deny any further continuances. In particular, we note that a two-year continuance is an ample period of time to prepare for a merits hearing on a cancellation application, and for the respondent to have changed attorneys only the day before the rescheduled hearing suggests to us a lack of appreciation on his part for the nature of the proceedings and his responsibility to prove his eligibility and worthiness for the discretionary relief of cancellation.
 
In regard to the matter of yet another continuance, the respondent admitted to the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 51) that “I acknowledge before you that a great part of all this has been negligence for not getting in touch with an attorney. I think I don’t deserve an opportunity.”
 
The respondent has, on appeal, alleged that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has accused former counsel of dishonesty. A complaint form against former counsel has allegedly been mailed to the State Bar of California.
 
Ineffective assistance of counsel is a denial of due process only if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491 (9th Cir. 1986). A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by: 1) an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; 2) a showing that counsel in question has been informed of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond; and 3) a showing that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, or if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent has not provided sufficient evidence to support a remand for further development of his charge of ineffective assistance. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). According to the transcript (Tr. at 42, 47-48), former counsel personally appeared before the Immigration Judge on the day of the hearing, September 18, 2003, and reported that he had been unable to communicate with the respondent because, among other reasons, the respondent’s telephone had been disconnected. Former counsel reported to the Immigration Judge that he had been trying to contact the respondent prior to the hearing but had been unsuccessful. Former counsel also stated to the Immigration Judge that his letters to the respondent had gone unanswered. It is apparent to us that the respondent’s cooperation with his then-counsel was less than he would have us believe. The respondent admitted (Tr. at 48) that, even had there been a communication problem, it was his responsibility to contact his counsel.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted voluntarily to depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails so to depart, he shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by DHS, he shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 3187276 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE REFUGIO IBANEZ ROSAS JOSEFINA OROBIO ROSAS
Files: AXX XX1 961 - Los Angeles
AXX XX1 432
December 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Meredith Brown, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening, cancellation of removal
 
The respondents appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated July 28, 2003, denying the respondents’ applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The respondents’ appeal also seeks to have their case reopened and remanded to the Immigration Court for additional proceedings. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the male respondent’s (AXX XXX XXX) application for cancellation of removal because the respondent failed to establish that his removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to a qualifying relative. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. The Immigration Judge pretermitted the female respondent’s (AXX XXX XXX) application for cancellation of removal because she failed to establish that she had been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of not less than 10 years immediately preceding the date of such application. See section 240A(b)(1)(A) of the Act.
 
On appeal, the respondents assert that they essentially received ineffective assistance of counsel from an immigration consultant and they have new evidence in support of their application for cancellation of removal. Likewise, they assert that they were deprived of due process because they did not have counsel and the Immigration Judge did not assist the respondents sufficiently to allow them to establish their eligibility for cancellation of removal. Moreover, the male respondent claims that he established exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his United States citizen children. Since the respondents have requested that their case be reopened and they have submitted evidence in support of their claim, we will construe their appeal both as an appeal and as a motion to reopen.
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999); see also Mohsseni Behbahani v. INS, 796 F.2d 249 (9th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 In this case, there is no evidence that the respondents were ever represented by counsel prior to the filing of their appeal on August 26, 2003. A Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative before the Board of Immigration Appeals (Form EOIR-27) was not filed until after the Immigration Judge rendered a decision in the respondents’ immigration hearing. While the respondents attempt to equate assistance from an immigration consultant to ineffective assistance of counsel, their argument is not persuasive. The male respondent’s affidavit does not establish what exactly the agreement was with the immigration consultant and what exact representations were made. The respondent’s affidavit does not indicate if the consultant had been hired to obtain work authorization for the respondent or whether he had actually hired the consultant to pursue an application for cancellation of removal. The male respondent’s affidavit also does not indicate that the respondent received any guidance or advice from the immigration consultant during the course of the respondents’ removal proceeding. Rather, the male respondent blames the immigration consultant for his being placed in removal proceeding. As such, the male respondent’s affidavit fails to conform to the first prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
The respondents also provided a letter addressed to the immigration consultant stating that it will be filing a complaint against the consultant. However, the letter is deficient. It fails to advise the immigration consultant of any details regarding the complaint. Instead, it just indicates that the advice resulted in deportation proceedings. The letter was allegedly sent on May 17, 2004, and the respondents’ appeal brief was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on May 19, 2004, only 2 days after the letter had been sent. As such, the immigration consultant was not afforded an opportunity to respond to the letter as required by the second prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
In an apparent attempt to comply with the third prong of the Lozada requirements, the respondents have submitted a document titled “County of Los Angeles Department of Consumer Affairs.” However, the document is in the Spanish language and is not accompanied by a certified English translation as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.33. Thus, it cannot be considered as evidence demonstrating compliance with the third prong of the Lozada requirements. Furthermore, the Order of Permanent Injunction does not establish that the respondents filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. In fact, the document makes no reference whatsoever to the respondents. Accordingly, the respondents have failed to comply with the Lozada requirements. Consequently, the respondents’ motion does not warrant reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 The respondents also submitted evidence along with their motion in support of their previously adjudicated application for cancellation of removal. A motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
In further support of their motion, the respondents have submitted a report card and two medical records. Academic records for the respondents’ children could have been presented at the former hearing. In fact, the Immigration Judge specifically advised the respondents that they could provide the school records for the children as early as July 30, 1998 (Tr. at 11). Therefore, the respondents had 4 years to submit evidence of the children’s school records. Moreover, a report card from a single grading period, absent any academic analysis, does not establish that the child has a learning disability, especially since the male respondent indicated that the children were doing well in school (Tr. at 46). Likewise, the two medical records reflect dates from 1999. Accordingly, the documents were previously available. In fact, the Immigration Judge continued the respondents’ case to allow them to provide additional medical records (Tr. at 24). Thus, the respondents have failed to establish that the evidence was not previously discoverable at their prior hearing and the motion will be denied. Id.
 
On appeal, the respondents essentially claim that they were denied due process because they were not represented by counsel and the Immigration Judge did not provide sufficient assistance to the respondents. The argument is without merit. The respondents were advised of their right to obtain counsel on several occasions and provided a list of legal services as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(a) (Tr. at 2, 12, 55). In fact, the respondents’ case was continued over a period of 4 years during which time the respondents could have acquired the assistance of an attorney. The respondents were also provided an interpreter throughout the proceedings and indicated they understood the interpreter (Tr. at 1, 4, 16, 18, 24, 26, 30, 33, 36). In addition, the Immigration Judge repeatedly advised the male respondent of the evidence that he should submit to support his claim for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 11, 16, 21, 24, 27). In fact, the Immigration Judge continued the case at least six times, over a period of 4 years, to allow the male respondent an opportunity to submit evidence in support of his application. Not only did the Immigration Judge afford the respondent an opportunity to present evidence, the Immigration Judge specifically advised the respondent of the type of documentation needed to support his claim, such as school records, tax records, employment records, and medical records (Tr. at 10, 11, 16, 21, 24, 27). As a result, the respondents were not denied due process or prejudiced by the Immigration Judge.
 
*4 The Immigration Judge’s decision regarding the female respondent’s application for cancellation of removal is not erroneous. An applicant for cancellation of removal must establish that she has been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of not less than 10 years immediately preceding the date of such application. See section 240A(b)(1)(A) of the Act. In this case, the female respondent entered the United States on March 15, 1989 (Tr. at 8). She was personally served with the Notice to Appear (NTA) on March 26, 1998. The period of continuous physical presence ended on March 26, 1998, when the NTA was served. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act. Thus, the female respondent’s period of continuous physical presence was for less than 10 years. Consequently, the female respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the male respondent’s application for cancellation of removal because the male respondent failed to establish exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to his United States citizen children. Cancellation of removal is available to an alien who has been physically present in the United States for at least 10 years, has been a person of good moral character, has not been convicted of a specified criminal offense, and has established that removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to the alien’s spouse parent or child, who is a United States citizen or lawful permanent resident. See section 240A(b) of the Act. The respondent shall have the burden of establishing that he is eligible for the benefit and that it should be granted in the exercise of discretion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). An applicant for cancellation of removal must demonstrate that his removal would result in hardship to his qualifying relatives that is “substantially different from or beyond, that which would normally be expected from the deportation of an alien with close family members here.” See Matter of Andazola-Rivas, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA 2002). Only hardship to qualifying relatives may be considered. See Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001). Factors to be considered are the ages, health, and circumstances such as serious health issues or compelling special needs in schools of the qualifying relatives. Id. Economic detriment alone is insufficient to support a finding of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship. See Matter of Andazola-Rivas, supra, at 232. All hardship factors should be considered in the aggregate. See Matter of Monreal, supra, at 64.
 
Upon review of the record of proceedings and the Immigration Judge’s decision, we do not find that his decision is clearly erroneous. In this case, the male respondent testified that his children have no health problems and that they are doing well in school (Tr. at 46). He testified that the children are not in any special programs (Tr. at 46). All the children speak Spanish and Spanish is the language spoken primarily at home (Tr. at 46). The male respondent owns a taxi valued at approximately $2,000 but does not own any real property (Tr. at 46). The children have received Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC), food stamps, and Medi-Cal (Tr. at 47-48). The respondent’s entire family resides in Mexico which consists of his parents and 13 siblings (Tr. at 51, 58, 59). He also testified that he might be able to get some assistance from his family in Mexico if he returns (Tr. at 51). Thus, the respondent failed to establish that his removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship. See Matter of Andazola-Rivas, supra; see also Matter of Monreal, supra. Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of cancellation of removal for both respondents.
 
*5 ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reopen and remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to so depart, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 3187272 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOFIA KORNEEVA
File: A73 175 541 - New York
December 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vladislav Sirota, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on July 22, 2004, when this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. On October 15, 2004, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance by her former counsel.1 The motion is denied.
 
In Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir.2000), the court held that “¿fºor an untimely claim to receive the benefit of equitable tolling … an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll.” “In a situation where fraud or concealment of the existence of a claim prevents an individual from timely filing, equitable tolling of a statute of limitations is permitted until the fraud or concealment is, or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.” Id., at 134.
 
In her motion for reopening, the respondent apparently alleges that her former counsel failed to adequately inform her of her hearing date, and that she received the notice late. However, the record reflects that in her motion to reconsider before the Immigration Judge, she alleged that she missed her hearing because she misread the hearing date, which conflicts with her current claim that she received her notice late. Moreover, the respondent has not stated what fraud or concealment prevented her from filing a timely motion. Our decision was issued on July 22, 2004, and she does not allege that she did not receive the decision. In our decision, we held that the respondent’s misreading of the notice did not constitute exceptional circumstances excusing her presence at the hearing. Consequently, at that time, the respondent should have become aware of a potential claim of ineffective assistance by former counsel. She has made no attempt to show that she exercised due diligence, and therefore we find she is not entitled to receive the benefit of equitable tolling.
 
Moreover, in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), we laid out the requirements that the respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. First, the motion should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts. Second, before the allegation is presented to the Board, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion. Finally, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements set out in Lozada. Because we find support insufficient, we will not entertain this claim.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2952329 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DOLORES PEREZ-MURILLO
File: AXX XX5 387 - San Diego
December 7, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kanwal Singh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Office of the Chief Counsel
San Diego
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 3, 2004. The pending motion was filed late, on August 27, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent seeks to have proceedings reopened in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status, based on marriage to a United States citizen. See section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1245. In Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), the Board held that a properly filed motion to reopen for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of proceedings may be granted in the exercise of discretion, notwithstanding the pendency of a visa petition filed on the alien’s behalf, where: (1) the motion to reopen is timely filed; (2) the motion is not numerically barred by the regulations; (3) the motion is not barred by Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), or on any other procedural grounds; (4) clear and convincing evidence is presented indicating a strong likelihood that the marriage is bona fide; and (5) the DHS does not oppose the motion or bases its opposition solely on Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992). Other than being filed untimely, and opposed by the DHS for that reason, the respondent’s motion appears to have satisfied these requirements. We note that the visa petition was approved on October 27, 2004.
 
The respondent argues that the motions deadline should be tolled, based on former counsel’s failure to inform her of the Board’s February 3, 2004, decision. The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent submits an affidavit in which she states that former counsel did not tell her of our decision. Under the circumstances, we agree that the motions deadline should be excused. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004)(motions deadline subject to equitable tolling where lawyer’s deceit caused late filing, and respondent thereafter acted with due diligence).
 
*2 Accordingly, the record is remanded in order to afford the respondent an opportunity to present her application for adjustment of status to the Immigration Judge.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2952329 (BIA)
End of Document

2004 WL 2952216 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANUEL JESUS QUIJADA-AVILA
File: AXX XX9 296 - Los Angeles
December 3, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard F. Lemus, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed out of time. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal without opinion on January 26, 2004. The respondent has filed an untimely motion on August 31, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent claims that former counsel failed to notify him of the Board’s decision so that he could either file a timely motion to reopen, or voluntarily depart under our January 26, 2004, order. However, the respondent has failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, those cases require, among other things, that the respondent give the attorney the opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffectiveness. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004)(notification to counsel is “required” for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Here, former counsel was not given an opportunity to respond to the claim that he failed to notify the respondent of our January 26, 2004, decision.
 
Even were the motions deadline tolled, it would be denied. The respondent claims that former counsel was ineffective, because his error resulted in the respondent not filing a timely application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(b)(1). Yet the respondent is not currently eligible for such relief, based on the information in the pending motion and proposed application for relief. The respondent claims that his removal would result in hardship to his United States citizen “child”. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) (applicant for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) must show hardship to qualifying relative). Yet a birth certificate for the respondent’s son indicates that he was born on February 10, 1982, and he is therefore 22 years old. To be considered a “child” for purposes of cancellation of removal, the relative must be under 21 years old. See section 101(b) of the Act. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA JUANA MENDEZ-ALVAREZ
File: AXX XX2 345 - San Francisco
December 2, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Resendez Guajardo, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. A final order of removal was entered by an Immigration Judge on October 6, 1998, in which the respondent was found to be ineligible for cancellation of removal. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal summarily on April 9, 2002. Over 2 years later, on September 28, 2004, the respondent has filed a motion to reopen. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The pending motion to reopen was filed very late. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s former counsel, Miguel Gadda, has been disbarred from practicing before the Board, as well as in California and in the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 857 (9th Cir. 2004)(in that case, where a timely motion to reopen was found, the Ninth Circuit ordered the Board to reconsider its decision concerning whether the respondent was prejudiced by Gadda’s actions). Here, the respondent asserts that prior counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge. She further argues that we should have informed her prior to our April 9, 2002, decision that Gadda had been suspended from practice before the Board. Yet there is no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that she has acted with due diligence. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir.2003). The respondent makes no such showing. We sent our April 9, 2002, directly to the respondent at her address. There is no affidavit provided from the respondent, and no argument made in the motion, as to why the respondent waited 2 years to seek new counsel and file the pending motion. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

2004 WL 2952228 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIX RAMON CONTRERAS-LARGAESPADA
File: A14 701 083 - San Francisco
December 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James Todd Bennett, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jo Ellen Ardinger
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On March 17, 2004, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States in absentia, because he failed to appear for his hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on August 5, 2004, but that request was denied on August 13, 2004. The respondent, a 47-year-old native and citizen of Nicaragua, has appealed from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent’s original counsel appeared at a hearing on March 10, 2004, but the respondent did not appear, allegedly because he had car trouble. He had left a message on counsel’s telephone to that effect. Counsel was notified in person that the removal hearing was rescheduled for March 17, 2004. Counsel appeared on that date and informed the court he was unable to contact the respondent. He told the Immigration Judge that he had attempted to contact the respondent repeatedly both by mail and by telephone. Consequently, the respondent was unaware of the hearing. The Immigration Judge granted a motion to withdraw as attorney of record and ordered the respondent removed. The new attorney on the motion stated that previous counsel did not inform the respondent of the hearing and asked that the respondent be given an opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility and cancellation of removal.
 
As correctly indicated in the Immigration Judge’s decision, a Notice of Hearing given to an alien’s attorney of record is proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5. Actual notice to an alien is not required. Farhoud v. INS, 122 F.3d 794 (9th Cir. 1997). We, therefore, find that the respondent was properly notified of the hearing. The respondent has an obligation to stay in touch with the attorney in order to stay informed of his immigration responsibilities. If he had contacted his attorney at any time after the first missed hearing, he could have been told the hearing was rescheduled.1
 
Although the respondent has raised the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel, claiming that counsel failed to notify him of the hearing, the respondent has fallen far short of establishing “exceptional circumstances” for his failure to appear at the hearing. See section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(A). The respondent speculates that the first counsel may have sent the letter to the wrong address and telephoned the wrong number. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (procedural requirement established in Matter of Lozada, supra, need not be rigidly enforced); see also Ontiveros-Lopez v. INS, 213 F.3d 1121 (9 th Cir. 2000) The respondent has made no attempt to inform his former attorney of the accusation of ineffective assistance, and, therefore, we are not given the benefit of the attorney’s explanation of the circumstances which led to the in absentia hearing. The respondent has also offered no explanation for their failure to report this incident to the California bar. We, therefore, are unable to find that “exceptional circumstances” exist which justify the respondent’s failure to appear for his hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

1

He also alleges he did not receive the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal decision; however, this contention would support only the argument that the motion should not be dismissed as untimely.

2004 WL 2952236 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO SAMPERIO-LOPEZ
File: AXX XX4 729 - Los Angeles
November 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles (Chuck) Redman
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
This case was last before us on July 16, 2004. In that decision we summarily affirmed the decision of an Immigration Judge dated November 18, 2002, in which the respondent was found to have abandoned his claims for relief due to a failure to comply with a deadline to have his fingerprints taken and to submit an application for relief. See Tr. at 54-56. On August 23, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. For the following reasons, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
The respondent alleges that this case should be remanded based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent concedes he has not complied with the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 20 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has failed to file an affidavit detailing the relationship between himself and counsel. Id. at 639. He has also failed to file a complaint with the appropriate state bar. Id. In the absence of detailed information regarding the relationship between the respondent and counsel, it is impossible for us to properly evaluate the specific substance of the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim. The respondent has submitted a letter from counsel admitting blame for the failure to timely file an application. However, in the absence of a state bar complaint, and in the absence of an affidavit from the respondent, we find that the respondent has not met the procedural or substantive requirements for reopening of proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. This case can be distinguished from Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003), a case on which the respondent relies for the proposition that he does not have to meet the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. The circumstances described in Lo v. Ashcroft, supra, indicated that the respondents, who had been issued an in absentia order of removal, had done all they reasonably could to have their cases heard promptly. In the present case, the circumstances leading to the finding that the respondent had abandoned his application for relief include indications that the respondent himself had not done all he reasonably could do to file his application on time.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent testified that he was told by counsel that he had to submit fingerprints in connection with his application for relief and that he did not do so (Tr. at 55). The respondent was convicted of a serious crime and, in a case where the respondent’s ability to demonstrate rehabilitation and good moral character was a potentially dispositive issue, the failure to comply with the fingerprinting requirement was clearly a failure to meet a reasonable and significant deadline. This failure, which is not attributable to counsel, constituted a reasonable basis for a finding that the respondent had abandoned his application for relief. See generally Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100 (BIA 1979); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (if an application or document is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application or document shall be deemed waived).
 
Finally, we note that information regarding respondent’s counsel’s apology for not filing the required documents on time was a matter of record at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision (Tr. at 55). Counsel’s letter taking responsibility adds little to the facts already present in the record and, without more, is not the type of information that forms a basis for reopening. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 (a motion to reopen should state facts not previously available); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.

IN RE: BERZOSA-FLORES, JOSE

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated May 03, 2004. The respondent bases his motion to reopen on two grounds: first, he seeks reopening to present new evidence in support of his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; and, second, he asserts that he was denied a fair hearing due to the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel, Marcela Garcia Moreno. The respondent’s motion is denied. While the respondent now submits additional documentation to bolster his cancellation application, he has failed to demonstrate that these documents were unavailable at the time of his removal hearing. 8 C.F.R. §1003.2(c). These documents do not contain any new or previously unavailable or undiscoverable information. The medical condition of the respondent’s twin daughters was considered by the Immigration Judge (I.J. 13-14). Regarding the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, he has not complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). There is no evidence that he filed a bar complaint against Ms. Moreno. Moreover, he has failed to prove any prejudice resulting from his former counsel’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

IN RE: (RUSHAJ) BOCAJ, IRMA

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated October 24, 2002, and re-issue it. We note at the outset that this is not a motion involving a court order to re-issue our decision. Nor is this a motion claiming that there was an administrative error on our part which caused a defect in the service of our October 24, 2002, decision. The motion is untimely. The respondent contends that it is untimely because she had ineffective assistance of counsel from her last attorney, Mr. Patrick Salley. She alleges that Mr. Salley failed to inform her when we dismissed her appeal. As a result, she was unable to seek a timely petition for review in the Sixth Circuit Court of Appeals. In support of reopening, the respondent submits, among other things, the affidavit of family members and a copy of her grievance to the State of Michigan Attorney Grievance Commission. Her complaint was filed on September 29, 2004. We find that the respondent’s evidence does not meet the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for her to prevail in a due process challenge. Moreover, prejudice has not been shown. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2952273 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
In re: GUSTAVO E. AGUDELO NANCY A. AGUDELO ADRIANA AGUDELO
Files: A74 663 728 - Miami
A74 663 729
A74 663 730
November 30, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Lourdes Martinez-Esquivel, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on June 16, 2004, when we denied the respondents’ motion requesting reconsideration of our January 23, 2004, decision, in which we dismissed their appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision due to untimeliness. The respondents seek to appeal the Immigration Judge’s September 11, 2003, decision again as they filed a second Notice of Appeal on June 24, 2004. This appeal is also untimely as it was filed well after the 30-day filing deadline following the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.53 (2004). The respondents now move to reopen the proceedings due to ineffective assistance of counsel and to stay the orders of deportation. The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
We note at the onset that we denied the respondents’ motion to reconsider our dismissal of their untimely appeal, and as a result, we still lack jurisdiction over this matter and the Immigration Judge’s decision therefore remains final (Board’s January 23, 2004, decision). Even considering the respondents’ appeal as a motion to reopen, they are barred by the time limitations on such motions. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (stating that a 90-day filing deadline following the date of a final administrative decision applies to motions to reopen). Moreover, the subject-matter of the motion does not place it within any of the regulatory exceptions to that limitations period. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3).
 
Even assuming we were to grant reopening on the basis of equitable tolling, we do not find that the respondents have presented prima facie evidence that their failure to timely file was due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Although respondents appear to have satisfied some of the procedural requirements for such a claim, they have failed to establish that they were prejudiced by their former attorney’s actions. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assadd, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). The respondents allege that their former counsel failed to file a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s September 11, 2003, decision, and also complain of insufficient preparation on behalf of former counsel’s law office (Respondents’ Motion at 2). The respondents filed their appeal on November 21, 2003, over 1 month after the appeal filing deadline. We note, however, that the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has held that “prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings,” and that “¿wºhere counsel fails to file any appeals brief in the context of an immigration proceeding, effectively depriving an alien of an appellate proceeding entirely, there is a rebuttable presumption of prejudice.” See Dakane v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 371 F.3d 771, 775 (11th Cir. 2004) (citing Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (citing Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045 (9th Cir.2000))). While counsel’s untimely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision may create a presumption of prejudice, we find that any such presumption is rebutted by the respondents’ inability to demonstrate how the failure to file a timely appeal would have altered the Immigration Judge’s decision.1 See id. at 775-776.
 
*2 Moreover, even if we were to find that the respondents have demonstrated prejudice, courts have consistently held that a party seeking equitable tolling must act with due diligence throughout the proceeding. See generally Justice v. United States, 6 F.3d 1474 (11th Cir. 1993). After we rejected the respondents’ untimely appeal on January 23, 2004, which is their primary point of discontent, the respondents submitted the aforementioned motion to reconsider our dismissal on February 19, 2004. However, they apparently waited to file a complaint with the Florida Bar regarding their former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance until May 10, 2004 (Respondents’ Motion at 1). Thus, even after we rejected the Respondents’ appeal, their former counsel continued his representation at their request for over 3 months. The respondents also never informed the Board that they sought withdrawal of their former counsel. Therefore, even assuming that all of Lozada’s requirements, supra, are satisfied, we are unable to find that the respondents acted with due diligence after learning of the alleged ineffective assistance of their former attorney.2 See, e.g., Justice v. United States supra, at 1483 (stating that equitable tolling is not warranted where a plaintiff failed to act with due diligence).
 
We also note that the respondents have not shown why the execution of their deportation orders should be stayed and accordingly deny their motion for a stay of deportation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f). We further find that the new evidence they seek to have considered would not change the result of the Immigration Judge’s decision, and therefore reopening is not warranted. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Finally, we find that the circumstances presented in the motion are not “exceptional” as to justify a sua sponte reopening of the matter. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, for all the reasons discussed, the respondents’ motion to reopen is denied.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Order Vacated by Meity v. Gonzales, 2nd Cir., March 23, 2006
2004 WL 2952271 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RINTA CHANDRANA MEITY
File: AXX XX6 039 - New York
November 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Raymond Fasano, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Virna Wright,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reconsider
 
*1 In an order entered on July 9, 2004, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings, following our order of April 23, 2004, in which we dismissed her appeal. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our most recent order. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). A motion to reconsider must assert that, at the time of our previous decision, an error was made. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard. Id.
 
The respondent has presented no persuasive evidence or argument that we committed error of law or fact in denying her motion to reopen. The country information that the respondent submitted with her motion to reopen on May 24, 2004, clearly indicated that the subject of religion continues to be an emotionally charged issue in Indonesia, but it did not demonstrate significantly changed country conditions. The background information revealed that, as of 1990, approximately 10% of the approximately 230 million Indonesians were Catholics or Protestants, and that Christians held several high posts in the government. Episodic violence between Muslims and non-Muslims continued to be a concern, but the evidence did not establish that the respondent’s alleged fear of persecution is reasonable. Given the evidence of record, we do not conclude that we overlooked or mis-characterized the state of affairs in Indonesia, in light of the respondent’s claimed experiences and fears.
 
The respondent also alleges that we erred in concluding that she had argued that ineffective assistance of former counsel prejudiced her, in regard to her failing to understand the consequences of not availing herself of the grant of voluntary departure. However, at page 10 of her motion to reopen, at section “d”, the respondent, through counsel, stated that she should be given an opportunity to withdraw her previous attorney’s application for voluntary departure. At paragraph 29, the respondent stated that “her previous attorney never explained to her the meaning of voluntary departure.” Given those statements in the respondent’s own motion, we do not find it beyond the pale to have concluded that she was referring to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In any event, the respondent now characterizes her argument regarding voluntary departure as one of a due process violation, rather than ineffective assistance of counsel. In essence, the respondent claims that we erred in failing to conclude that the consequences of failing voluntarily. to depart were not explained to her prior to the Immigration Judge’s decision. It must be remembered that the respondent was represented by competent counsel at the proceedings, and that counsel requested voluntary departure on her behalf. It is reasonable to assume that competent counsel, in the performance of his duties, explained the aspects of voluntary departure to the respondent. If the respondent had any doubts about the nature of the privilege of voluntary departure, they were certainly dispelled prior to the conclusion of the hearing. The Immigration Judge explained voluntary departure to the respondent (Tr. at 67-68), and she responded that she understood the matter.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD
