2004 WL 2952242 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HUSAM E. H. ABU ASAB
File: AXX XX3 929 - Chicago
November 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert W. Dekelaita, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lynn K. Hollander
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Continuance, remand
 
The respondent timely appeals the Immigration Judge’s June 13, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s request for a continuance and ordering him removed. The respondent further submits a motion to remand. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The respondent’s motion requests a remand to provide the respondent the opportunity to adjust his status. Where, as here, a motion to remand is in the nature of a motion to reopen, the motion must comply with the substantive requirements of the latter motion. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). The governing regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) require that a motion to reopen “shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.” The regulations further specify that “¿aº motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.” Id.
 
The respondent has not complied with those requirements. The respondent merely provides a copy of his I-130 (Immigrant Petition for Relative) with the motion. That is not evidence that the respondent is admissible to the United States, i.e., that none of the qualitative grounds of inadmissibility apply in his case. That showing requires, at a minimum, a properly executed adjustment application. Inasmuch as the respondent has failed to provide an adjustment application, we will deny the respondent’s motion to remand.
 
The respondent claims on appeal that he received ineffective assistance of counsel throughout his proceedings before the Immigration Judge. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 Upon review of the respondent’s brief and the evidence submitted on appeal, we find that the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim fails for numerous reasons as it neglects to abide by any of the requirements provided in Matter of Lozada, supra. Accordingly, we will not remand the record to the Immigration Court to remedy the alleged ineffective assistance.
 
Finally, we note that the respondent argues on appeal that the removal proceedings were unconstitutional in that they usurped the Congressional power to legislate. The Board is not empowered, however, to rule on the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations that we administer. Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343 (BIA 1982).
 
Based on the foregoing, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny the motion to remand.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2952242 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2004 WL 2952269 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS ALBERTO MENDOZA
File: AXX XX9 316 - Los Angeles
November 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Johnson, Howard G., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). On appeal, the respondent renews a request for relief through cancellation of removal. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the equities presented fall well short of the strenuous standard we set forth in Matter of Andazola, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA 2002) and Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001)(qualifying relative must experience hardship that is “‘substantially’ beyond the ordinary hardship that would be expected when a close family member leaves this country” in order to meet the “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” standard). In addition, the respondent’s equities are also sufficiently distinct from those presented in Matter of Recinas, 23 I&N Dec. 467 (BIA 2002) that we find them insufficient to establish exceptional and extremely unusual hardship.
 
In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent has filed a motion to remand. The motion is denied. A motion will not be granted unless there is a reasonable likelihood of success upon reopening. See generally Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216, 1218 (BIA 2000) citing Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996); INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (indicating that motions to reopen in immigration proceedings are disfavored). Since we find that the qualifying relatives would not suffer the requisite hardship upon the respondent’s removal, the case would not appear to have a likelihood of success if proceedings were remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges previously receiving inadequate legal representation, the respondent failed to comply with the requirements we detailed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (laying out the requirements that an alien must meet in order to support a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). While we acknowledge that the record of proceedings contains a refund request filed with the California Department of Consumer Affairs, there is no indication that the respondent filed a complaint regarding the former representative with the appropriate legal disciplinary authorities. Id. at 639. We therefore find that the respondent has failed to establish that reopening would be warranted for ineffective assistance of the prior representative. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2952230 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALAN CASTREJON
File: AXX XX7 049 - Los Angeles
November 30, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Meltzer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
This matter was last before the Board on December 19, 2002, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and request for administrative closure. On April 27, 2004, the respondent filed the instant motion to reconsider pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion is untimely. A motion to reconsider must be filed within 30 days after the mailing of the Board decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). Here, the respondent did not file his motion until 16 months after the Board issued its decision. The respondent asserts in a supplemental filing submitted to the Board on October 14, 2004, that his motion is untimely because neither he nor his former attorney received the Board’s prior decision, and he was therefore unaware that his motion to reopen had been denied. The respondent asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled. The respondent has submitted new evidence in support of his claim of ineffective assistance. We will therefore treat his motion as one to reopen rather than one to reconsider. Compare 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (stating that a motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, supported by pertinent authority) with 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (stating that “a motion to reopen proceedings shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted and shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material”).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, has ruled that filing deadlines for motions before the Board may be equitably tolled where a respondent has been prevented from filing by deception, fraud, or error, as long as he acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1224 (9th Cir. 2002). In order to receive the benefit of equitable tolling, a respondent who alleges ineffective assistance must first comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The motion should be supported by an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, including a statement setting forth the details of the agreement that was entered into with former counsel. See id. at 639. The respondent should also notify former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance so that he or she may respond, and, if the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities on the part of former counsel, the respondent should file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Id. In this case, the motion is supported by an affidavit from the respondent, a copy of his contract of representation with former counsel, a complaint form for the State Bar of California dated October 10, 2004, and a copy of a letter to the respondent’s former counsel informing him of the complaint (Respondent’s Exhs. A-C). We find that the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. See Rodriguez-Lariz, supra, at 1226-27 (stating that the factors are not rigidly applied).
 
*2 The respondent’s affidavit states that neither he, nor his former counsel, received a copy of the Board’s decision regarding his original motion to reopen (Respondent’s Exh. A). The record reflects that the Board’s decision was sent directly to the respondent at his last known address because his former attorney failed to file a Form EOIR-27, Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative, despite being notified by the Board on August 26, 2002, that he needed to do so. The notice, which was also mailed to the respondent’s last known address, provides: “Unless a Form EOIR-27 is received from your representative, you will be considered pro se (representing yourself) before the Board and all future notices, including the Board’s decision, will be sent directly to you at your address above and not to your representative.” The respondent’s affidavit states that he moved in January 1998 and that his former counsel was aware of his new address and sent correspondence there; however, the respondent speculates that his former attorney “never notified ¿ the Boardº of the change of address.” The respondent states that he and his wife called former counsel periodically regarding the status of his case, and they were always told that it was pending, even “¿aºs late as April 13, 2004.” The respondent says that his wife sent a letter to the attorney in May 2004 informing him that she and the respondent were dissatisfied with his service and planned to file a complaint with the California Bar Association (Respondent’s Exh. C, undated). The attorney apparently did not respond to the letter.
 
We do not find that the respondent acted with due diligence regarding his case and conclude that he does not qualify for equitable tolling. First, we note that it is the alien’s responsibility to ensure that his address of record is current. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(e) (requiring that within 5 working days of any change of address, an alien must provide notice to the Board via Form EOIR-33). We further note that although the respondent filed his original motion to reconsider in April 2004, around the time he allegedly discovered his former attorney’s wrongdoing, he did not assert his claim of ineffective assistance until 6 months later, and did not file a complaint with the California State Bar until October 2004. The respondent has provided no explanation for these delays, except that he is a long-distance trucker and spends much of his time away from home.
 
Finally, even assuming the respondent received ineffective assistance, he has not shown that his case was prejudiced by the errors of former counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 901-02. Even if the respondent’s motion to reconsider had been timely, it did not identify errors of fact or law in our previous decision. Thus, assuming that he had received our decision in a timely fashion and filed his motion to reconsider immediately thereafter, the respondent’s motion still would have been denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS FLORES-PELAYO
File: AXX XX9 770 - Portland, Oregon
November 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Meltzer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas L. Day
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the September 9, 2003, decision of the Immigration Judge, denying waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c), and we also adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge dated October 2, 2003, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge correctly denied waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) due to the seriousness of the aggravated felony offense for which the respondent pled guilty and was sentenced to 8 years imprisonment. Moreover, the report provided by the evaluator, Richard King, Ed. D., notes that, although the respondent seemed to have benefitted from sex offender intervention, he “must continue to be restricted with his contact with female teenagers.” We do not find that the evidence shows adequate rehabilitation if the respondent still needs to be restricted from contact with young females. Further, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that the basis for reopening cited by the respondent primarily related to testimonial issues and that the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel would not change the outcome and there is no indication of any prejudice based on the representation the respondent received. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2952178 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIE NICOLE ILESTIN
File: AXX XX1 648 - Miami
November 22, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered orally on August 20, 2004. The appeal was accordingly due on or before September 20, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on October 4, 2004. We find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent filed a Motion for Late Filing of Brief in which the respondent raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as the reason for the untimeliness of her appeal. Counsel’s September 24, 2004, letter, which returned the respondent’s partial payment of $500.00 for failing to pay the filing fee for an appeal in full and copies of payment receipts made to counsel were provided as evidence to support this claim. However, the Board need not address this issue considering the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Therefore, we do not find this factor to be an adequate reason to accept this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Flores-Alvarenga v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., August 1, 2006
2004 WL 2952153 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DOUGLAS NEIL FLORES-ALVARENGA
File: AXX XX8 839 - Los Angeles
November 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Celeste S. Castro, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nadia Fleischer,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 18, 2004, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. In his motion, the respondent sought reopening claiming ineffective assistance of former counsel. Specifically, the respondent alleged that his former counsel failed to file an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation under 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 3367 (BIA 1998).
 
In addition, we note that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 Based on the actions of the respondent’s former counsel and the respondent’s compliance with Lozada, we find that he established ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent did not provide any evidence with the motion which indicates that he would have prevailed with his appeal, had one been filed. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was prejudiced by his former attorney in these proceedings. Consequently, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2952296 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GONZALO HERNANDEZ-MONTELONGO MARISOL GUZMAN-ALEARO NOEMI HERNANDEZ-GUZMAN
Files: A75 769 431 - Los Angeles
A75 769 432
A75 769 433
November 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
John C. Nelson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Remand; cancellation of removal
 
On April 7, 2003, an Immigration Judge found that the respondents had abandoned their applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), but granted their request for voluntary departure. The respondents have appealed from this decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondents are a family from Mexico. On appeal, they contend that they were the victims of ineffective assistance of counsel during their hearing before the Immigration Judge, and they therefore ask that their cases be remanded to the Immigration Judge to allow them to apply for cancellation of removal.
 
To prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must show that his or her counsel’s performance was ineffective, and he or she must meet the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The alien also must establish that he or she was prejudiced by his or her attorney’s actions. See Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel is a due process violation and that due process challenges to deportation proceedings require a showing of prejudice to succeed) (citations omitted). In this case, the respondents have not met the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and they also have not shown that they were prejudiced by their attorney’s conduct.1
 
According to Matter of Lozada, supra, an alien must (1) submit an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the alien in this regard, (2) notify counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned of the allegations leveled against him or her and give him or her the opportunity to respond, and (3) show whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities. In this case, the respondents have not presented any evidence to show that they have informed their former counsel of the charges against him and given him the chance to respond to these charges.
 
*2 The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, has held that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the Board does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada.” See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (citations omitted). Nevertheless, the Ninth Circuit has stated that the Lozada requirements are not sacrosanct. Id. Specifically, the court has found that, when the facts are plain on the face of the administrative record, the requirements of Lozada “are not dispositive.” Id. In reaching this conclusion, the court explained that “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.” Id. at 526.
 
The record in this case suggests that the respondents may bear some of the responsibility for the failure to meet the deadline for filing their applications for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 13). And the adult male respondent’s brief statement regarding his former counsel’s inaction does not clarify this point. Accordingly, notice to the respondents’ former counsel is necessary in this case to establish that their former attorney has had the opportunity to present his version of the facts and to demonstrate that the respondents’ complaint is legitimate and substantial. Cf. Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, supra (finding substantial compliance when record on its face showed ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondents’ failure to submit evidence that they provided this notice therefore prevents us from concluding that they are entitled to a remand to seek cancellation of removal.
 
In addition, the respondents have not shown that they were prejudiced by the actions of their former attorney. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that alien is prejudiced if attorney’s conduct may have affected the outcome of the proceeding). The adult female and the young female respondent have not shown that they meet the continuous physical presence requirement of section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, and the adult male respondent has not submitted evidence suggesting that his removal will impose exceptional and extremely unusual hardship on his United States citizen son.
 
The adult female and the young female respondent apparently entered the United States on November 25, 1992, but they were served with the Notice to Appear on May 22, 2001. They therefore did not obtain 10 years’ continuous physical presence before their period of continuous physical presence ended. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act.
 
*3 The adult male respondent does appear to have accrued 10 years’ continuous physical presence before he was placed in proceedings. He, however, has not submitted evidence to suggest that his removal would impose any hardship beyond the normal hardships imposed by removal on his United States citizen son. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra (finding that alien had not established prejudice when alien had not offered evidence to suggest that he met the extreme hardship requirement for suspension of deportation). Given these facts, we cannot conclude that the respondents are entitled to a remand to apply for cancellation of removal, and we dismiss their appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security. See section 240B(b) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to so depart, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the Department of Homeland Security, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The respondents in this case have raised their ineffectiveness of counsel claim on appeal rather than in a motion to reopen. The respondents, however, are still seeking remand, which requires the reopening of their proceedings, and they are still required to meet the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, or to show substantial compliance. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (indicating that strict adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required).

2004 WL 2952142 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LORENA LEAL-ZAVALA
File: AXX XX2 570 - Phoenix
November 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kevin Gibbons, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Arthur S. Raznick
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A) (i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
We adopt and affirm the June 13, 2003, decision of the Immigration Judge pretermitting the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The record of proceedings reflects that the respondent departed the United States under a threat of removal in February 1998 (Exh. 5). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that such departure interrupted the period of the respondent’s continuous physical presence in the United States. See Vasquez-Lopez v. Ashcroft, 343 F.3d 961 (9th Cir. 2003); see also Matter of Romalez, 23 I&N Dec. 423 (BIA 2002). As the record fully supports the result of the Immigration Judge’s decision, we affirm the conclusion that the respondent failed to demonstrate eligibility for cancellation of removal.
 
The respondent has raised on appeal a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, essentially consisting of her prior attorney’s failure to question the “voluntariness” of the respondent’s February 1998, departure. However, other than advancing the aforementioned allegation on appeal, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements necessary to establish a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. See Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2004); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the Department of Homeland Security, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2952146 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMATOULIE ABDOU FYE
File: AXX XX4 679 - Baltimore
November 19, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irena I. Karpinski, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda A. Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before us on February 2, 2004, when we dismissed as abandoned the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s order denying her motion to reconsider. The respondent now moves that we reconsider our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider has been filed out of time. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 2, 2004. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2), a motion to reconsider in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 30 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, the Board did not receive the respondent’s motion until August 6, 2004, which is clearly more than 30 days after that order. The respondent’s motion to reconsider has therefore been filed out of time. On October 4, 2004, the respondent filed a reply to the DHS’ opposition to her motion to reconsider. However, such reply does not cure the lateness of the respondent’s motion.
 
The respondent claims that she fell victim of ineffective assistance of her prior counsel who represented her during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge, and who filed on her behalf an appeal before this Board. The respondent claims that her prior attorney failed to timely file an appeal from an Immigration Judge’s order denying her application for relief from deportation. She also claims that the attorney failed to file an appeal brief with this Board. Finally, she claims that her prior attorney failed to promptly notify her of our February 2, 2004, order dismissing her appeal as abandoned, thus preventing her from filing a timely motion to reconsider. However, the respondent has failed to give her prior attorney notice of the nature of her complaint against him, and to offer him an opportunity to respond. Thus, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements necessary to establish a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, a review of the record of proceedings reflects that our February 2, 2004, order was mailed on that date to the respondent’s last address of record. As such, the respondent has failed to keep us appraised of her current whereabouts. Accordingly, we find it inappropriate to reopen proceedings.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2952069 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BELAY WOLDEMARIAM
Files: A70 502 880 - San Francisco
November 18, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Rochelle A. Fortier Nwadibia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Phillip D. Hatfield
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide wilt those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). An Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on April 1, 2003. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he never received the notice of hearing from his former attorney, because she sent the hearing notice to the wrong zip code for the respondent. He also asserts on appeal that he is eligible for adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen.
 
A review of the record reflects that the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on April 1, 2003. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on February 11, 2004, claiming that he did not receive notice of the hearing. On February 25, 2004, the respondent filed a response to the Government’s opposition to respondent’s motion to reopen, in which he claimed that he had provided his former attorney with his change of address in August 2000. However, the change of address provided by his former attorney contained an incorrect zip code for the respondent’s address. The respondent also submitted a declaration in support of his motion to reopen. Consequently, the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance of counsel in his response to the Government’s opposition.
 
The respondent failed to file his motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the final order, as is required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act).1 However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has determined that the statute of limitations to reopen an order of deportation is equitably tolled where the alien’s late petition is the result of deceptive actions by a representative. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999). Furthermore, we have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, supra.
 
*2 Precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit provides that while the requirements of Lozada are generally reasonable, they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 n.6 (9th Cir. 2000). The Ninth Circuit determined that “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra. Furthermore, where the face of the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel, there has been substantial compliance with the rule. Id.
 
We find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that on July 5, 2001, notice of the hearing was mailed to the respondent’s former attorney of record. Consequently, the respondent received notice of the hearing in accordance with the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Furthermore, the respondent failed to comply with all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent submitted an affidavit with his response to the Government’s opposition to his motion to reopen, and on appeal he has submitted a copy of a complaint he states was filed with the State Bar of California. However, there is no evidence that his former counsel was informed of the allegations of ineffective assistance and given an opportunity to respond. As noted by the Immigration Judge, the ineffective assistance of counsel claim is based solely on an erroneous zip code, which may or may not have been supplied to her by the respondent. If the respondent’s former attorney had been informed of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, any response from her may have shed light on the origin of the erroneous zip code. This is not a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel that may excuse strict compliance With the Lozada requirements. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004) (court found that alien had not substantially satisfied Lozada when he failed to submit an affidavit and could not prove that he gave former attorney notice of the ineffective assistance allegations or an adequate opportunity to respond). Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear.
 
*3 We will next consider the respondent’s claim on appeal that he is eligible for adjustment of status. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).2 Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).3 Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision. He is accordingly precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.4 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

We note that Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings pursuant to section 242B of the Act. However, as section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act sets forth identical language to that contained in section 242B(c)(3), we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, by analogy.

2

We note that Matter of M-S-, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings and involved the interpretation of sections 242B(e)(1) and (5) of the Act, relating to the limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a deportation hearing. 2004 WL 2943597 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BORIS OSWALDO MEDINA-HERRERA
File: A29 331 428 - New Orleans
November 18, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul S. Zoltan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Nonimmigrant - remained longer than permitted
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 24, 2004, decision denying his motion to reopen and reconsider a December 17, 1999, order denying his prior motion to reopen proceedings to pursue relief under section 203 of the Nicaraguan and Central American Relief Act of 1997, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193, 2196 (“NACARA”), amended by, Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On October 30, 1990, the respondent was ordered deported in absentia. On September 14, 1998, the respondent, through prior counsel, filed a motion to reopen to pursue NACARA relief. Because the respondent did not file a complete application by the statutory deadline, the Immigration Judge denied the motion on December 17, 1999, ruling that the respondent had not establish prima facie eligibility for relief. On February 5, 2004, the respondent, represented by new counsel, filed a motion to reopen and reconsider, alleging that his prior attorney rendered ineffective assistance when she submitted an incomplete application for NACARA relief. The Immigration Judge denied as untimely that motion on February 24, 2004. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to equitably toll the filing period.
 
While the respondent is correct that the period for filing a motion to reopen or reconsider may be equitably tolled under certain circumstances, the respondent has not established that he is entitled to equitable tolling in this instance. See e.g., Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001) (en banc); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). Even if prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance in failing to submit a completed application by the statutory deadline, we nevertheless find that equitable tolling is not applicable because the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in seeking reconsideration. See Ivoraski v. INS, supra, at 134; Pacheco v. Rice, 966 F.2d 904, 906-07 (5th Cir. 1992) (“Equitable tolling is applicable when, despite all due diligence, a plaintiff is unable to discover essential information bearing on the existence of his claim.”). More than 4 years passed from the Immigration Judge’s December 17, 1999, decision, and the respondent’s February 5, 2004, motion to reopen and reconsider. While the respondent asserted in his complaint to the State Bar of Arizona, alleging inadequate representation by his prior counsel, that “it seemed odd” that he had not received any correspondence from his attorney after the November 1999 filing, he waited several years before inquiring on the status of his case. Under these circumstances, we find that he did not exercise due diligence in exercising his rights. Thus, the Immigration Judge properly denied his motion to reopen and reconsider as untimely.1
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The respondent also argues that his February 5, 2004, motion was not a motion to reconsider but rather a motion to reopen his 1990 in absentia deportation order, and asserts for the first time on appeal that the 180-day time limit for rescinding an in absentia order did not apply because he did not receive adequate notice of the October 30, 1990, hearing. While we decline to consider this notice issue because it was not raised before the Immigration Judge, we note that the Order to Show Cause reflects personal service on the respondent (Exh. 1). Moreover, even if we were to agree with the respondent that his 2004 filing is more appropriately a motion to reopen the in absentia deportation order, rather than a motion for reconsideration, it would still be untimely, since we have ruled that the 180-day time limit may not be equitably tolled even if the failure to timely file can be attributable to ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998).

2004 WL 2952096 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HEE J. CHANG
File: A38 650 139 - Newark
November 17, 2004
 
IN EXCLUSION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF APPLICANT: Pro se1
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on September 30 1996, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s March 25, 1996, decision finding that she had abandoned her application for relief under the former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), because she had failed to timely file the application. On August 20, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen the proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The record reflects that at a January 4, 1996, hearing, an Immigration Judge set a February 23, 1996, filing deadline for receipt of the respondent’s application for section 212(c) relief. Although the respondent’s counsel submitted supporting documentation on February 23, 1996, the application was not filed at that time. In the brief filed in support of the respondent’s appeal to the Board, the respondent’s former counsel conceded that he had thought that the cover letter and supporting documentation submitted by him on February 23, 1996, comprised the request for section 212(c) relief and was unaware that an Application for Advance Permission to Return to Unrelinquished Domicile (Form I-191) was required. He stated that he had attempted to file the application on March 26, 1996, but was informed that the file was closed. The record contains a cover letter dated March 22, 1996, in which the respondent’s former counsel states that he is submitting the necessary application and forms for the respondent, apologizes for the delay, and states that he was unaware an application needed to be filed.
 
The respondent has filed a motion to reopen the proceedings almost 8 years after the Board rendered its decision dismissing her appeal. The regulations specify that a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, the deadline for motions to reopen may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001) (en banc); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2d Cir. 2000). An individual seeking to rely upon equitable tolling of a filing deadline must show that she has acted with due diligence. Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, supra; Iavorski v. INS, supra.
 
In the instant case, the respondent missed the filing deadline by more than 7 1/2 years, a substantial amount of time. However, the respondent alleges that her former attorney not only failed to timely file the section 212(c) application which she had completed and sent to him prior to its February 23, 1996, due date, but that he failed to notify her of the Immigration Judge’s decision, of his filing of an appeal on her behalf with the Board, and of the Board’s dismissal of the appeal. Additionally, she claims that her former counsel deceived her over the years by coming up with various excuses as to why her case was still pending. For example, he told her that a law had been passed shortly after her conviction precluding convicted felons from applying for section 212(c) relief but that the retroactive application of this law was being contested by a number of attorneys, that she was not an illegal immigrant because her case was still pending, that the courts had told him her documents were too old and they were looking for them, that the courts were trying to pull her Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) report to see if she was still living as a good citizen, and that he had sent a letter to the Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS) asking about the status of her case. In October 2000, she applied for a reentry permit through and on the advice of her former counsel because she had planned to travel out of the country, but was informed that such application was denied because she could not provide proof that she was a lawful permanent resident. In September 2002, the respondent’s former counsel told her that she could again submit an application for section 212(c) relief in order to get closure in her case, and the respondent sent him a completed application and supporting documentation a month later. When she later questioned him about the application, he informed her that her case should be resolved in the spring of 2003, and she was later informed that her case should be resolved that summer. The respondent finally consulted another law firm about her case in February 2004. In June 2004, an attorney informed her of her former attorney’s failure to timely file the section 212(c) application and of the outstanding order of exclusion and deportation entered against her, and this motion to reopen was filed in August 2004.
 
*2 The respondent in the instant case complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and demonstrated that the order of exclusion and deportation entered against her was the result of having received ineffective assistance of counsel. A July 21, 2004, letter sent to the respondent’s former counsel details the above-mentioned facts and informs him that a motion to reopen will be filed with the Board based on the ineffectiveness of his representation. The Board has received no response from the respondent’s former counsel regarding the respondent’s allegations of ineffectiveness in failing to timely file the application and then engaging in a course of deceit and misrepresentation over a number of years in an apparent attempt to conceal the initial mistake. The record also reflects that a complaint was filed with the appropriate attorney disciplinary committee.
 
We find that equitable tolling is warranted in the instant case. Although the respondent missed the filing deadline for a motion to reopen by several years, this was due to her former counsel’s misrepresentations regarding the status of her case. We find that the respondent exercised due diligence in filing the motion shortly after she discovered her attorney’s malfeasance. We note that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the respondent’s motion. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court to allow the respondent to pursue an application for section 212(c) relief and any other form of relief for which she may now be eligible.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with this decision.

Bailey, it was not accompanied by a Notice of Entry of Appearance as the Attorney or Representative before the Board of Immigration Appeals (Form EOIR-27). A courtesy copy of this decision will be sent to Ms. Alfaro.

2004 WL 2952089 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE DE JESUS CABANAS OBREGON
File: AXX XX5 535 - Los Angeles
November 17, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Simon Salinas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 17, 2004, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. In his motion, the respondent sought reopening claiming ineffective assistance of former counsel. Specifically, the respondent alleged that his former counsel failed to file an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s February 4, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation under 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 3367 (BIA 1998).
 
In addition, we have considered that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent has not informed former counsel of the accusations against him or filed a complaint with the bar association against his former attorney. Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003)(as the alien provided no evidence that the prior counsel received the notice of the motion to reopen and did not give the prior counsel an opportunity to respond to the allegations, the court held that the alien failed to comply with Lozada’s notice requirement).
 
The respondent also did not provide any evidence with the motion which indicates that he would have prevailed with his appeal, had one been filed. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was ineffectively represented or was prejudiced by his former attorney in these proceedings. Consequently, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

IN RE: DURGA SINGH

IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994)(noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that, while equitable tolling is available for untimely motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 See e.g. Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). The Immigration Judge also correctly determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status was filed untimely. Furthermore, only the District Director has authority to extend the time within which to depart voluntarily specified initially by an Immigration Judge or the Board. 8 C.F.R. § 240.26(f). Consequently, we find that the respondent’s arguments presented on appeal are without merit. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2943510 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NARINDERJIT SINGH
File: A73 419 674 - San Francisco
November 15, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vinay R. Chari, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(A)º - Excludable at entry under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i) - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of material fact
Sec. 241(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(A)º - Excludable at entry under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - No valid immigrant visa
 
Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on January 20, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Previously, on March 24, 2003, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent on August 6, 2004, filed an additional motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
An alien may file only one motion to reopen deportation proceedings, and the motion must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The instant motion represents the respondent’s second attempt to reopen his proceedings and was not filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. The respondent requests that the filing deadline and the numerical limitation be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his appeal before the Board.
 
The doctrine of equitable tolling may, in certain circumstances, override the rules regarding motions to reopen in cases where the respondent has been defrauded by an individual purporting to provide legal representation. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1224 (9th Cir. 2002). In order to receive the benefit of equitable tolling, a respondent who alleges ineffective assistance must first comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that the Lozada requirements are “not sacrosanct,” the Ninth Circuit has also held that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the BIA does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 Here, the respondent submitted a copy of a complaint to the State Bar of California, a copy of a letter sent to his prior attorney, and an affidavit regarding the alleged ineffective assistance of his prior attorney. However, the respondent’s affidavit does not describe “in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken … and what counsel did or did not represent to the respondent in this regard.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Rather, the respondent describes very generally his agreement with his attorney regarding his appeal before the Board. Specifically, the respondent states that his attorney “assured ¿himº that he would file an appeal on ¿hisº behalf.” The respondent does not indicate whether his prior attorney agreed only to file a Notice of Appeal or whether he also agreed to file a brief on the respondent’s behalf.1
 
In the absence of an explanation of the representation agreement between the respondent and his prior attorney, we cannot determine whether the attorney failed in his duties to the respondent. In this regard, we note that this case does not involve “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” so as to excuse the respondent from satisfying the formal requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. Castillo-Perez, supra, at 525-26. We therefore find that the respondent failed to show that he received ineffective assistance from his prior attorney.
 
Furthermore, even if we found that the respondent demonstrated that he received ineffective assistance from his prior attorney, we would find that there is no basis for abrogating the motions deadline or numerical limitation here. An individual seeking to rely upon equitable tolling must show that he acted with due diligence. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). Here, the respondent apparently learned of the Board’s dismissal of his appeal in March 2003. However, he did not seek a second opinion about his case until May 2004, shortly after he received a “bag and baggage order” from the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). In the interim, the respondent’s prior attorney represented him in a motion to reopen before the Board and in an appeal to the Ninth Circuit. The respondent’s receipt of the “bag and baggage order” from the DHS did not stem from the alleged ineffective assistance of his prior attorney before the Board (not filing an appeal brief).2 Rather, the receipt of the “bag and baggage order” appears to have spurred the respondent to action over a year after the Board’s dismissal of his appeal, when the respondent claims to have received ineffective assistance of counsel. We therefore find that the respondent’s case does not merit equitable tolling. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the respondent’s prior attorney included detailed arguments on the Notice of Appeal and that, because of this, we did not summarily dismiss the respondent’s appeal despite the fact that his prior attorney did not file a brief.

*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on February 5, 1998, when we administratively closed these proceedings based on the Presidential directive to implement deferral of enforced departure for certain Haitian nationals. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has now requested that the proceedings be reinstated because the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 902 of the Haitian Refugee Immigration Fairness Act was denied, on January 8, 2002, because the respondent failed to submit evidence needed to establish his eligibility for HRIFA relief. The respondent did not respond to the request for reinstatement. Accordingly, we will grant the DHS’s request for reinstatement and we will adjudicate the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 1992, denial of his motion to reopen.
 
On August 17, 1992, the respondent was granted an extension of time within which to submit an appeal brief, but he did not submit a brief. In 1999, his counsel filed a motion to withdraw his representation because the respondent failed to stay in contact with him or to otherwise cooperate in seeking relief from deportation. The Immigration Judge’s decision fully addressed his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and determined that a claim was not established under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Gbaya v. U.S. Attorney General, 342 F.3d 1219 (11th Cir. 2003) (reopening was properly denied where there was not substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements). On appeal, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WAGUENER CASSAMAJOR
File: AXX XX0 846 - Orlando
November 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ifeoma A. Uche, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Yon Alberdi,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 22, 2004. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on April 3, 2000. The respondent filed a pro se motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on June 24, 2002. The Immigration Judge granted the motion, reopened proceedings and scheduled another hearing for November 14, 2002. The notice of decision and hearing was mailed to the respondent at the address he provided in his motion to reopen. The respondent failed to appear at his November 14, 2002, hearing and the Immigration Judge entered another order of removal in absentia. On or about March 5, 2004, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen in which he addresses his absence from the April 3, 2000, hearing. With the motion, he included a copy of his ineffective assistance of counsel complaint filed with the Florida Bar against the counsel who represented him during his April 3, 2000, proceedings. The respondent did not address in any fashion his absence from the November 14, 2002, hearing. Consequently, the Immigration Judge was correct in denying the motion because the respondent was notified of the November 14, 2002, hearing and failed to explain his failure to appear.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2943478 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHIANG JEN CHEN
File: A73 632 5421 - New York
November 12, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan Lee, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 19, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before May 20, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until October 18, 2004. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
The respondent is currently incarcerated for not complying with the terms of voluntary departure granted her by the Immigration Judge. She requests that the Board take her case on certification (or sua sponte) and reopen proceedings on our own motion, claiming the voluntary departure instructions given by the Immigration Judge to her parents and herself were misleading and indicated that the restrictions surrounding a failure to comply with the voluntary departure conditions applied only in the event of arrest and removal from the United States. While the Board retains limited discretionary powers under the regulations to reopen or reconsider cases on our own motion, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), that power allows the Board to reopen proceedings sua sponte only in exceptional situations. In this case. even if we accept the respondent’s quotation of the Immigration Judge’s instructions as correct, we find that, especially in light of the respondent’s representation by counsel at the hearing, the argument regarding confusing instructions is not sufficient to warrant reopening.
 
In an addendum to the respondent’s motion to reopen, she makes a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, though we note the respondent has not complied with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We note also, for an untimely motion to reopen to receive the benefit of equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the alien must exercise due diligence in pursuing the case during the period to be tolled. The final administrative order was issued by the Board in February, 2002. Inasmuch as the respondent has waited over 2 1/2 years before filing the instant motion, she has not exercised due diligence in pursuing remedies available to her with regards to the alleged ineffective assistance violations.
 
Accordingly, he motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s motion is captioned “IN THE MATTER OF CHEN, Shiang Jen (A73 632 542/A99 151 453).” Alien registration number A99 151 453 does not appear to be a valid number.

2004 WL 2943591 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUIZ CARDENAS, FRANCISCO IGNACIO
File: AXX XX1 508 - Los Angeles
November 10, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raul Gomez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on December 19, 2003, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s September 19, 2002 decision that the respondent had waived his opportunity to be considered for cancellation of removal because the application was not filed by the required date. We note that the respondent was represented at that hearing by Terrence McGuire, Esquire. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen proceedings on September 7, 2004 alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. According to the respondent, Mr. McGuire was negligent in his representation, and also was working in conjunction with an individual unauthorized to practice law. The motion is denied. The respondent’s motion is untimely because it was filed more than 90 days after the date in which the final administrative decision by the Board was rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has also held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time and numerical limitations for motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir.2002); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir.1999). In the present case, the respondent was aware on September 19, 2002 that Mr. McGuire did not file his application for cancellation of removal on time, and therefore may have been the victim of attorney negligence. Having waited until September 7, 2004 to raise this issue establishes that he failed to pursue his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with due diligence. We therefore see no reason to equitably toll the 90-day statute of limitations for seeking reopening before the Board.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
